A Descriptive Damage Hack for Dungeon World/World of Dungeons

\n
\n
\n
\n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
\n
\n\nMarch 2014 \n edited March 2014 in Game Design Help
\n
\n
\n
\n
\n I've had a hankering for some dungeon-crawl-type gaming, and thinking about different ways to do this using something Powered by the Apocalypse. One thing that's always left me unsatisfied with games like Dungeon World, however, is the take on damage and wounds: subtracting hit points from a running total seems like a particularly unexciting way to do things for a game which cares a lot about colourful, in-fiction action. However, hit points (and diminishing hit point totals) are definitely part of D&D-style gaming. So how can we use them in a fun, more modern way?

This is my basic idea, still unplaytested. I love some things about it and yet I also have a feeling there might be something missing. I'm going for a feeling of danger and uncertainty as well as a visceral connection to the fiction, and a vivid focus in the details of what's happening "on screen". I'd love to hear what you think. (Or, better yet, try this in your game and let me know how it goes.)

The basic premise:


*When You Get Hurt*

You miss a roll or something bad happens and suddenly you're hit by something. This is a scary moment! The MC describes the delivery of the injury and makes clear the danger but leaves the precise effect of the injury on you unstated. The possible extent of the damage should be pretty clear, however: have you potentially lost your arm or been run through? This should sound scary and suspenseful, with the character's livelihood hanging in the balance. Here's what this looks like:\n

"The troll's hammer comes down with incredible speed and hits your head: there's a sound like a thunderclap and the next thing you know, all you're seeing is red."

"The creature's jaws close around your torso and bear down, rotten teeth the size of kitchen knives piercing your corselet with a sickening crunch."

"The spinning blades come down on your arm at the last moment. You can feel them pull you to the right; worse yet, there's blood everywhere."

"Diving under Aragorn's blow with the speed of a striking snake, the orc-chieftain charged into the Company and thrust with his spear straight at Frodo. The blow caught him on the right side, and Frodo was hurled against the wall and pinned."
\nBy default, an injury throws you completely off-balance (due to shock, impact, etc.), will hinder you if you're continuing to fight (should you be able to), and could cause serious problems for you after the fight's over. So, it has an instant effect, a continuing effect throughout the remainder of the fight, and will still remain a danger (or even get worse) after the encounter is over (bleeding, infection, etc).

If the fictional circumstances are such that one of these is not possible, congratulations: you're getting off easier than that. But, generally speaking, this is what you're dealing with when you're injured.

It also may or may not threaten immediate death (more on this below).
\n

    \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
    \n
    \n\nMarch 2014 \n edited March 2014
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n When you get hurt, you can (and should) choose to try to resist the harm. You're a hero, after all:\n
    When your character suffers a horrible injury or wound , you may choose to resist harm. Roll+number of hit points you'd like to spend. One a hit, choose options. On a 13+, all of them. On a 10+, choose 2. On a 7-9, choose 1.
    * This injury doesn't hamper you, you can ignore it for now
    * You got lucky: you'll find later that the injury's not as big a deal as it seemed
    * You maintain your position or advantage, and can react immediately (if you do, take +1 forward)
    On a miss, whatever else the MC says happens, you suffer the injury in full, right now.
    \nThere's no minimum hit point expenditure: you may always choose to spend 0 hit points. However, the 13+ category here exists mainly as an incentive to spend at least one hit point every time you make this roll.

    When you've suffered any dangerous injuries and you have a moment to gather yourself, you can try to recover from them (with thanks to @Johnstone for the basic idea for this move):\n
    When you fight to recover from trauma, roll+number of hit points you'd like to spend. On a 10+, you recover quickly and as fully as possible under the circumstances. On a 7-9, you will recover but the MC chooses one:
    * It will take extra time or require specific conditions (e.g. bed rest, an antidote to a poison, smelling salts)
    * You need outside help (perhaps skilled medical care)
    * You find yourself in a tight spot or unpleasant situation
    * It marks you in a permanent way
    On a miss, whatever else the MC says happens, you do not recover.
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n

    \n

    \n
    \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
    \n
    \n\nMarch 2014 \n edited March 2014
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n So, for the sake of our example, when Frodo gets run pinned to the wall of the cave, he rolls to resist harm and gets a 7-9. He chooses "You got lucky: you'll find later that the injury's not as big a deal as it seemed". He's incapacitated for the moment and unable to do anything, but when his companions inspect him after the fight, it turns out that his mithril armour saved him.

    After the fight, Aragorn is carrying him away when he comes to. He's probably still in a lot of pain, however, so he rolls to recover. Here he manages a 10+, and gets back on his feet with no trouble. It may hurt, but he's a sturdy hobbit and he can grit his teeth and keep going.

    \n

    \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
    \n
    \n\nMarch 2014 \n edited March 2014
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n How does a character die under these rules? If you fail a "resist harm" roll, or succeed but choose options which mean you are potentially dead, you've been mortally injured (or perhaps you're already dead, and we just don't know yet!). You're lying on the battlefield, bleeding out, or still fighting but on your last legs. Now it comes down to the roll to recover: if you fail this one, you're a goner.

    This means getting injured is scary, but the odds of you dying from any particular injury are not as high as you might think. Someone with no hit points left has (approximately) a 17% chance of dying in a fight where they get seriously injured once (cumulative odds of rolling two misses). If you have any hit points to spend at all, your odds are better, of course.

    In any case, these rules make it clear what the nature of hit points truly is: hit points are a sort of buffer between you and death, giving your character a chance to survive dangerous situations, but they do not mean anything specific. You can lose hit points but avoid most of a blow, or vice-versa. There's no longer any ambiguity here about what they stand for or what they represent.


    Sidenote: If I was using these rules with Dungeon World, or a similar game which gives characters a lot of hit points, I'd consider treating them as *lifetime* hit points. Or, perhaps, allowing them to replenish very slowly (for instance, you get half of your class's bonus hit points - 2 for a Wizard, 5 for a Fighter - each time you take an advance).

    If you want to always keep death on the table, no one should be allowed to spend more than 4 hit points on any given roll.
    \n

    \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
    \n
    \n\nMarch 2014 \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n The main design question here is:

    How do we decide when an injury is potentially lethal?

    I can see some solutions, but I'd like to hear yours, as well.

    * The MC decides when an injury is potentially lethal. If the fiction says it's something that could kill you, it can.
    * All injuries are assumed to be potentially lethal by default; the roll will determine whether this is the case or not.
    * We institute some kind of roll or other procedure to determine whether a source of damage is potentially lethal or not.

    For instance, we can add another MC-side roll into the equation, and give each monster a "lethality" rating, from 1 to 5. When a character gets hit by an attack, the MC rolls 2 dice before describing the injury.

    * If one die rolls a result equal to the lethality rating or lower, the attack is potentially lethal.
    * If both dice roll a result equal to or lower than the lethality rating, the attack is potentially instantly fatal.

    This is nice because it gives a nice range of odds for what we want, and feels AW-like (rolling 2d6).
    \n

    \n
    \n There's a lot here to take in and I might have more to say on a second read. Here's a first impression, though, which I hope is helpful. My interests currently lie in the same direction as yours\u2014gritty fiction-first dungeon crawling, powered by the Apocalypse. But, speaking only for me and my tastes, I really want to simplify! It looks like you're moving in the opposite direction, toward more complexity. For my part, what I'd want is a lot less mechanics and a lot more prompts and advice on using MC moves effectively to address different combat outcomes. Eg. Put them in a spot: like prone on the ground! Take away their stuff: like their weapon or their arm! Announce future badness: like a festering wound\u2014time to start a countdown clock!

    \n I'm not big on spending hit points and I'm not big on killing PCs (just want to make that clear), but if you've done all that's honest and possible and the character still fails that final roll, here's what to do: Sit there silently for a moment, then look somberly at the player, and address him by his character's name: "Boromir... What happens when you die?"

    \n
    \n My reading is that getting hit does NOT deal Hp damage- it has fictional consequences only. So the player thinks about how badly they wanna avoid this blow and then stakes some Hp on the roll to avoid it. Hit or miss, that Hp is gone. This is a really cool idea!

    If I'm reading that correctly, then maybe a dinky little goblin hits you with a stick and you're like, "whatever, goblin. I'll save my Hp and roll +0 for the harm move." But then his big Cave Troll friend smashes you with a spiked club and you're all, "woah woah woah, this could kill me, I'll give up six Hp to survive it..." This is cool and I feel like it emulates a very real expenditure of energy and attention: the bigger the threat, the more exhausting it will be just to fight it. When you spend all your Hp avoiding the worst effects, you are tired, worn out, and have little left to give during the next battle.

    A character could theoretically always spend 0-1 Hp and rely on luck to survive, which seems fun and alright. Or they could strategically conserve Hp for the most fictionally dire hits coming their way. It is more complicated, overall, but it's a very neat idea.

    \n I've been mulling this over for a few hours since I first read it. I think I like it. Keeping hit points maintains the resource-manipulation (as opposed to just doing everything purely in narrative) aspect of a game system. Of course, it needs to be played to see if it's smooth in use.

    \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
    \n
    \n\nMarch 2014 \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n 1. Yes, you spend hit points (or not) every time you roll a harm move, so they no longer represent your physical state. You could burn all your hit points in be in perfect health, or have all your hit points and still die.

    This makes hit points a resource, and spending them to survive encounters is a strategic choice you have to make. You can suffer through injuries now and risk death in order to save them for when it counts, or try to stay safe *now* in hopes that things will be better later. This is potentially very interesting, especially if your hit point pool is a lifetime supply: once your hero's "plot immunity" runs out, it may be time to retire (or face an increased risk of death, and hope that your experience and improved abilities are enough to handle what you're dealing with now).

    That could potentially create an interesting contrast between "brand new" adventurers and veterans, who have more skills and experience but less "beginner's luck" when it comes to getting hurt.

    2. @CarpeGuitarrem: that's my question with this hack, the thing I'm wondering about. Is it enough? I'd say it's pretty clear that the consequences of losing your arm are quite different from ingesting a deadly poison. But maybe it will feel unsatisfying in play.

    With standard hit points (as in AW), there's a big difference in granularity (3-harm is clearly a different deal from 1-harm), whereas here that is potentially lost. However, under these rules there's a great variation in the type of damage, instead: every injury is different and will affect in different ways. In AW, taking 2-harm from a hail of arrows is no different from taking 2-harm from a flamethrower or because something bit your leg. It's still the same number.

    Here, they will all be very different. So, the quantitative difference in injury is less apparent, but the hope is that the qualitative range of harm comes to the forefront instead.

    3. @creases: simplicity is always the goal, I think. I'd say that what I've outlined here is at roughly the same level of complexity as Dungeon World or Apocalypse World, or maybe a tiny bit simpler, but significantly more complex than World of Dungeons. However, there's also a great deal of flexibility built-in to a "resist harm" and "recover" move (it's easy to apply these moves to situations hit points couldn't handle, like concussions, mental issues, emotional trauma, broken legs, etc.). If you have suggestions on simplifying this further, I'd love to hear them!


    ...

    For comparison's sake:

    Dungeon World
    -Must track character hit points
    -Must track monster damage
    -Weapon and armor modifiers
    -Healing rules (including the "Recover" move, sleeping, making camp)
    -One "death" move (Last Breath)

    This Hack
    -Must track character hit points
    -Injuries must include detailed description
    -One "harm" move
    -One "recovery" move
    -Optionally, a monster harm rating and/or roll
    \n

    \n So, I think my sticking point here (because I love the idea of qualitatively differentiating harm) is that the quantity of harm is, itself, a quality. So it doesn't quite feel right to give it all the same weight.

    What if it was closer to "for each point of HP spent" vs. "for every two points of HP spent" vs. "for every three points of HP spent" to differentiate normal (a goblin), massive (a troll), and absolutely brutally horrendous (a great big colossal monster like a giant dragon) hits? Because I feel like a description of the injury isn't quite as effective at differentiating the distinction between being stabbed by a knife and being smashed by a giant hammer.

    (I really like the HP-spending mechanism.)

    \n I'm not 100% sold, but I think it's very interesting. @Carpeguitarrem: I think the qualitative difference is in the fictional risk. Like, with this system it'd be legit for the player to say "wait, before I spend Hp and roll, what's the worst that could happen to me?" And the MC answers truthfully: "the ogre will probably break your arm" or "the henchman's blow will bruise you" or "the dragon may bite you in two."

    Then, the player decides how much Hp she's willing to stake on avoiding this. So she could spend 4hp to roll +4 when a trapped chest threatens to cut off her fingertip, or roll +4 when a mighty giant threatens to rip her arm off. It's the same +4 and the same expenditure, it's a question of when and how you save those points and what each wound is worth to you.

    It's fun and interesting and highlights the hp-as-a-resource aspect pretty well. There are downsides, though. Like, players probably will be nervous about spending Hp on any minor wound because they're afraid of being at the mercy of the dice later. This could lead to some awkward nickel-and-diming of someone's health and then a series of "nothing can hurt me because I'll spend 6+ Hp on every roll" situations. It also makes PCs less heroic, because instead of shrugging off mighty blows until the very end, they're getting excruciating detail on every little bit of harm they receive. It trades off the quick cinematic harm rules for a nuanced in-the-character's-shoes view of things.

    Have you considered Paul Taliesin's AW harm moves? It seems like they could be ported to DW easily.
    \nHa! Assuming you were talking to me: yes, that was me. (I am Paul Taliesin.) Just exploring some other options here!

    I feel that those rules are better suited to something like Apocalypse World (where the characters are protagonists in a story and injury is a frightening thing, but you can always survive if you want to keep playing that character). This is a shot at doing something which could work in more of a dungeon-crawling mode (characters can and do die, we don't necessarily want to keep track of every little injury, and we want the system to be objective enough that the MC doesn't have to make any awkward choices but can act as impartial referee).

    \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n

    \n\nMarch 2014 \n edited March 2014

    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n@Scrape: yes, you've got the gist of it.

    I wouldn't use rules like this in a game where you'd expect to get hurt 5 or 6 times in any significant confrontation. "Lord of the Rings" was one of my inspirations here, and so the ideal gameplay would look something like that. Most people go down after one solid hit, or maybe shrug off the first one but go down after the second one.

    But in those moments where you're willing to spend your precious hit points, you can have a moment of glory. That's Boromir at the end of the Fellowship: he's getting hit by arrows by he just keeps going, spending his life-force to protect the hobbits.

    Remember that when you get hit by something... you've definitely been injured. If you roll well, it just means you're able to soldier through and ignore the injury, like the hero of an action movie. You grit your teeth and you just keep fighting like nothing happened.

    (Just like hit points in D&D: you're still getting hurt, it's just that you're able to ignore the damage.)

    As for how many hit points you can regain and how often, that's a dial to play with. The more hit points you have, the less easy it should be to regain them. Maybe if all you've got is 1d6 hit points, you regain them all after each significant action scene, or whenever you rest. But if you've got lots of hit points (as in Dungeon World), they should be really hard to regain, so you can't just spend a ton of them each time you get hurt. (That's why I came up with the "lifetime hitpoints" idea, just as a thought experiment.)

    It's just a question of balancing how often you expect to get hurt with how many hit points you should be able to spend without running out. Depends on what kind of game you're playing and how many hit points we're talking about!

    As for hoarding: I don't particularly see any problem with this, so long as the characters' lives are under threat. If you want to be a hoarder, you might not survive very long... and when you do, you'll be the guy with the broken leg that your friends are carrying on the stretcher. You like to live dangerously! Fine, cool by me. But maybe you're right: that's something to check with playtesting!

    \n Firstly, I really like the idea you've come up with here. I might consider using it next time I play DW. I still have some questions though.

    Where does armour come into this? I was thinking it could increase the total hit points, or increase the rate of hit point recovery? I think it would be a bit overpowered to give it a bonus to your resist damage roll, but then again armour is pretty overpowered in DW already.

    \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
    \n
    \n\nMarch 2014 \n edited March 2014
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n Ah! Yes. Great point. Armour is one of the things I've been wondering about, and it doesn't slot into this terribly easily.

    Here are some options I've brainstormed so far:

    * Armour is handled narratively. It'll protect you under certain circumstances, like deflecting arrows fired at long range, but a serious attack that's already been described as successful is still dangerous as ever.
    * For someone to hurt you, they need a fictionally convincing way of getting past your armour.
    * This could be generalized for literary/on-screen effect: the first time the armour comes into play, it effectively stops an attack, but the next one will be treated normally.
    * Armour has its own small pool of hit points, which can be spent when relevant (e.g. you can't spend hit points from your chain mail when someone stabs you in the eye). You replenish them by repairing the armour.
    * Armour, when relevant, gives a +1 bonus to the resist harm move. If it should protect you completely, +2.
    * If you're using some kind of mechanic or roll for the lethality of monster attacks, the armour comes into play on the MC's side. With my suggested 2d6 for monster lethality, armour means the MC rolls 3d6 and discards the lowest die, or heavy/magical armour means you roll 4d6 and discard the two lowest dice.


    EDIT: It's also possible to simply abstract away armour just like DW already does it with weapons. As a Fighter you deal d10 damage because you're badass and you probably have big weapons. Similarly, then, if you have lots of hit points it's because you're badass and probably wear heavy armour.

    \n Gotcha; I somehow was still thinking of normal DW Hp totals and picturing the hoarding going on. Makes more sense with less, for sure. Also, hearing that it's specifically the "non-heroic" version makes it click even harder. I like this idea quite a bit!

But I like the concept and general math, which I might use wholesale. Cool!

    \n Paul, I think the most elegant solution for armor is simply +1 or +2 ongoing on the Harm roll.

    ...actually, you know what? Armor would be a fantastic way to solve that issue I've had with this hack. When you're hit by the attack, the GM tells you to take +something forward to the harm roll. That +something depends on what your armor is and what the attack is. A troll hammer might mean that your plate armor gives you +0 forward, while a goblin spear might let you take +2 forward on the roll. A fire elemental might make you take -1 forward, of course, against any armor...

    \n
    \n My only concern at this point is how to integrate it with class moves that interact with the damage and HP components of the system. (That, and do you just damage monsters like normal?)
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n

    @CarpeGuitarrem,

    If you're using this hack with Dungeon World, you'll want to keep using damage and hit points for monsters unchanged, which should allow you to use all (or the vast majority) of the class moves.

    (I'm not a huge fan of the mechanical damage-add moves, myself, but they're there, so if you're playing DW, you gotta deal.)

    \n
    \n
    \n\nJohnstone \n\n\n
    \n
    \n\nMarch 2014 \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n In the Frodo example, I kind of feel like the "you got lucky" option is kinda double-dipping or whatever. If you choose that one, you shouldn't need to make the recovery roll later. The way that scene plays to me is that he suffer a hit and the effects of it, and later rolls the recovery move, maybe with a bonus for the mithril and rolls really well.

    For reference, these are the harm rules in Throne of Dooms (a medieval fantasy drama game that I haven't finished yet):

    When you suffer harm, you can choose to give in or resist. If you give in, instead of rolling, take the wound as it was intended and mark experience. If you die, make a new character and take an advance right away.

    \u25cf If you resist the harm, roll+dangerous. On a 10+, the wounds are cosmetic, superficial, and don\u2019t hinder your actions. On a 7-9, the wound is crippling in some way. You lose the ability to use a limb or sense, either permanently or temporarily. On a miss, the wound is mortal. Create a countdown with steps equal to your will+1. Each time you take action without dealing with your wound first, mark a step. Also mark another step if you are injured again. When they are all marked (or if you have will-1), you die.

    \u25cf If you are resisting harm by recovering, roll+dangerous. On a 10+, you recover quickly and as fully as possible. On a 7-9, you recover either quickly or fully, but not both. On a miss, you do not recover. If the wound is mortal, you do not survive. If the wound is not mortal, it either refuses to heal or leaves you debilitated when it does.

    If you have an advantage on your roll (armour, medicine, etc.), you can get a +1 to your roll.


    And the rules I'm using for the game of Traveller with AW stuff for resolution that I'm running:

    Harm numbers are pretty much the same as in AW. Roll harm in dice and keep the two highest (1 harm = roll 3 dice, keep the two lowest). On a 10+, the wound is mortal. Create a countdown with steps equal to 1+endurance. Each time you take action without dealing with your wound, mark a step. When you mark all steps (or if you have endurance-1), you die. On a 7-9, the wound is crippling. On a miss, the wound is cosmetic, but you might still fall over or drop something.

    So basically, the same as above but reversed, and with actual harm ratings.
    \n

    \n Johnstone,

    I like those! Very nice. I'm glad some good stuff came out of our earlier conversations on this topic!

    Looking over your moves; I guess that means there's no way for someone to be killed "on the spot"? (I was trying to keep that option because I was thinking of using something like this in a somewhat OSR-like mode of play.)

    Why do you change the number of dice for the harm roll in your Traveller game (as opposed to dice+adds, or something else)? Is it an aesthetic choice, or something particular you're looking for with the distribution? Also, who rolls?

    As for Frodo's near escape, I agree with you. I suppose the question here is whether a) the wound is "hampering" him even after the fight's over, and b) whether he's fighting to recover from other effects (like having been knocked unconscious or whatever). I like your take on it, though - that might be a better way to deal with it.

    \n\nJohnstone \n\n\n

    \n\nMarch 2014 \n edited March 2014

    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n There's no way to die instantly after the dice come out, no. In Throne, the player can take the xp for a fatal blow, and their next character starts with an advance. And there's always narrative death, where there's no chance in the fiction of the character surviving. But people dying instantly from violent attacks is more cinematic than realistic, so I'm cool with giving PCs the chance to reach medical attention while they are bleeding to death.

    Multiple dice is from Black Seas of Infinity, which is semi-related. Stats are numbers of dice, you get penalties and bonuses through a few limited means. Anybody can roll, it doesn't matter who.

    \n\nAsIf \n\n\n

    \n Here's something I'm tinkering with. This would be an alternate move for "Last Breath" in those last seconds of a character's life (i.e., this move is not mandatory, but may be performed if the player chooses).

    THE DYING MOVE
    When you are Breathing Your Last Breath, you may choose to roll+ your highest stat. On a 10+, the GM will select one event from the list below, and you select another. On a 7-9, you may select one.

    - Final Words
    - Controlled Collapse
    - Curse your Enemy
    - Inspire your Friends
    - Watch from Beyond
    - Transcending Vision
    - Sudden Understanding

    Final Words = you speak a short soliloquy for which combat time is momentarily halted
    Controlled Collapse = you decide how your body falls (this may be of tactical or symbolic value).
    Curse your Enemy = select an enemy and hold a single -3 against it, to be played when you decide
    Inspire your Friends = anyone with a Bond for you receives a +1 for the duration of this scene
    Watch from Beyond = hold a single +3 for a selected friend, to be played whenever you decide
    Transcending Vision = a vision of the cosmos/heaven/hell, a communication from your deity
    Sudden Understanding = the GM tells you a secret that provides a new perspective. This could be the Impending Doom of an active Front, the meaning of a Portent, a revelation of the enemy's strategy, a glimpse into the mind of an NPC, etc.

    Interestingly, this solves a problem we've had in the past where as GM I really didn't care a lot about HP on the player end because it got in the way of what we were doing in the game. This incentives players focusing on positioning in a very particular and careful way. Lots to think on here.

    \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
    \n
    \n\nMarch 2014 \n edited March 2014
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n Great; it would be fun to get some playtest of this in action. Please report back in this thread, and let us know how it goes!

    My latest thinking on this:

    1. If you're playing a game where hit points are sparse (like WoD), you should be able to reroll your hit points pretty easily, like by resting and eating.

    For instance, let's say you have 1d6 hit points, and you roll a 4. You get hit a few times, and you have 1 hit point left. When you get back to safety, bandage your wounds, have a meal, you can reroll that 1d6 and write down the new result as your hit points (as long as it's higher than your current hit points; this shouldn't hurt you, of course).


    2. If you want to play Dungeon World, or a similar game with lots of hit points, either make hit points really hard to regain, or reduce the number of them.

    I recommend this:

    Option 1

    - If it's a long-term game, remove "natural healing" (after all, hit points aren't physical damage). You get half of your starting class hit points every time you score an advance (e.g. 5 hit points for a Fighter). Healing spells work normally, however.

    Option 2

    - If it's a short-term game, halve all hit point totals, rounding up. So a Cleric starting with 8+Constitution hit points has (8+11) 19 hit points? Now he has 10. Treat all the healing rules normally.


    3. For the lethality of wounds, assume that any wound which sounds like it could plausibly kill someone is lethal. Assume the worst, in other words, every time.


    4. For armour, pick one of the options below and let us know how it works for you:

    Option 1

    If you're wearing armour or protection which shields or protects you from a source of damage, you get to roll at +1 [like Armour 1 or 2 in DW]. If the armour or protection is designed to protect you completely against this kind of threat (e.g. plate mail against slingstones), so you're basically impervious to such an attack, make your rolls at +2 [like Armour 3 or 4 in DW].

    However, your opponents should be smart and switch to tactics which circumvent armour whenever possible. So you might get an easy time of it the first time they attack you, and maybe the second time, but after that they're likely to try something more clever. (Chain mail is susceptible to crushing weapons, for example.)

    This bonus should depend on your position and circumstances; it's not a simple bonus that applies all the time just because you're holding a shield and wearing chain mail or whatever.

    Option 2

    For each point of armour rating, give that item of protection a single checkbox. You can put a line through a box to take +1 on a resist harm roll when the item would logically protect you. You can use as many boxes as you like, and you can even put a second line through a box (making a "X") for another +1.

    After a fight is over, erase any single lines through boxes, but those with two lines (an "X", crossing out the box) stay - these are "spent" for good. This represents permanent damage to your armour, shield, or whatever. (But it can probably be repaired, if you have access to a blacksmith or some similar means of doing so.)

    So, a suit of scale armour (armour 2, weight 3) has two "boxes": [ ] [ ]. You could put a line through one box and cross out the second for a total of +3 to your roll: [/] [X]. After the fight, you can erase the line through the first box, but the second box is now crossed out for good: [ ] [X].
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n

    \n\nShinobi \n\n\n
    \n
    \n\nMarch 2014 \n edited March 2014
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n I'm planning on doing the following:
    *Dungeon World one shot adventure, to encourage buy-in and experimental mindset
    *Dungeon with multiple monsters, traps, and environmental hazards to encounter - different modes of injury to explore with description
    *Level 1 characters
    *HP pools are calculated normally and halved as above- no more HP until the module is over.
    *GMing: Will treat 'deal damage' move as 'injure characters'. Deadly moves on misses, maiming or injury on partial hits when applicable. Fiction first.
    *Armor applied 1:1 against passive checks, 1:2 against active defenses -trying to avoid 'gotchas' here, don't want a ten-foot pole session, so I'll give players the benefit of the doubt, but will include hazards that avoid armor (gas? crushing?).

    If someone else wants to try the other options, I'd love to hear how those worked out as well!

    \n I would like it better if you spent hit points after your roll to resist damage, each hit point adding 1 to your result. The situation of gambling hit points and still getting a bad result would be very unrewarding for me and make the resource feel flimsy instead of concrete and really useful. It would also make me feel a lot more reserved about spending them.

    I also agree that some monsters should be more dangerous then others. It makes the most sense to me to represent this on the resist harm roll as a minus to the roll, with more dangerous monsters having a higher negative modifier. A monster with a high modifier pretty much guarantees you'll be spending a lot of hit points if you go toe to toe with it.

    I also think one of the character's stats should play into this roll, similarly to defy danger, even though that would complicate the math a little more. Some characters should be better at resisting damage then others, right?

    \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
    \n
    \n\nMarch 2014 \n edited March 2014
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n Shinobi:

    Cool! I like the sound of that.

    Orion:\n
    [...] It would also make me feel a lot more reserved about spending them.
    \nWell, that's precisely the idea. We *want* the players to be spending smaller amounts of hit points, so the result of the roll remains interesting. (Ideally, we get 7-9 or 10+ results on most harm rolls.)

    Feel free to try it the other way, of course! It does remove any risk from getting hurt, though, since you can always choose how safe you are (until you run out of hit points, of course).

    If I have 10 hit points, I know that nothing can kill me, for example. Even if I roll a 2, I can spend 8 points to upgrade it to a 10+.

    I don't like that effect; I want things to be scary all the time. But maybe you do!\n
    I also agree that some monsters should be more dangerous then others. It makes the most sense to me to represent this on the resist harm roll as a minus to the roll, with more dangerous monsters having a higher negative modifier. A monster with a high modifier pretty much guarantees you'll be spending a lot of hit points if you go toe to toe with it.
    \nThe idea here is to keep to the basic logic of AW-based games, which is that in more dangerous situations your odds of success don't change... instead, it's what's at stake which becomes more serious.

    A truly dangerous monster will be harder to attack (like the 16-HP dragon, if you're familiar with that), and more likely to threaten injuries which are really horrifying. This means you'll be spending more hit points over time.

    For instance, a knife stab to the arm is pretty scary, but you'd feel fairly comfortable risking a bad roll. But when a monster grabs you with its prehensile tongue, pulls you into its mouth and chomps down on your helpless body, you'll probably want to spend some more hit points, no? Even setting aside the danger of death (although that's clearly the biggest factor here!), you'll probably be a lot more worried about not getting impaled on its teeth and being unable to do anything, and also more worried about getting a chance to react and maybe get away before it chomps you away. Both of those are more likely if you spend those hit points.

    That's my take on it, anyway. I could be wrong!\n
    I also think one of the character's stats should play into this roll, similarly to defy danger, even though that would complicate the math a little more. Some characters should be better at resisting damage then others, right?
    \nI think that, in most games with hit points, your stats already affect how many hit points you have. So this sounds like double-dipping to me. On the other hand, it could be a good opportunity for some special moves, which give a bonus to resist harm or to recover.
    \n

    Another option is including a ratio: maybe you can spend 1 hit point for a +1 before the roll, but after the dice have fallen, it just costs you 6 hit points to upgrade your result by one category. (I initially thought "2 points per +1" but that just seems like unnecessary complication here: might as well go with a "flat rate" for simplicity.)

    You could also limit the improvement after the roll to one category (so a miss can, at most, be upgraded to a 7-9).

    \n I'm interested in why we think DW is giving too many HP to play with for this system. If a character has 20 HP and they're being conservative; let's say they're spending 1HP on each roll.     A Descriptive Damage Hack for Dungeon World/World of Dungeons

      \n
      \n@ChristopherWeeks:

      You may be right about that! It depends if we're imagining a modern D&D-style fight, where you might get injured multiple times in a single fight and keep going (and, therefore, we expect drawn-out combats with multiple exchanges), or an OSR-style fight, where getting hit means you go down immediately, or, if you're lucky, you survive the first and drop on the second hit, instead (and we don't expect drawn-out fights, but rather brief potentially-lethal moments of terror).

      What's other people's experience?

      Realistically, if you want to be doing basically OK, you'll probably want to spend enough hit points for a +3 on each roll.

      The recover roll is maybe less critical, since it usually happens outside of combat (and probably only once after each fight).

      @akooser:

      What do you mean by "playing without HPs"? Can you explain? Sounds interesting.

      \n\nSevenSidedDie \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nMarch 2014 \n edited March 2014
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n This isn't actually so far off vanilla Dungeon World. I started reading this thread and I was actually confused, because "Doesn't DW already do this? That's how I already run the game." Completely moving away from HP to a more AW style of injury, with fleshed-out moves for it, is a nice fallout of the conversation though. Giving players a different relationship to danger (by taking away the HP security blanket) is a good way to tweak how play feels and emerges.

      (For anyone I'm confusing with "DW already does this", I'm referring to the Damage section in the beginning of the book:\n
      HP loss is often only part of the effect. If the harm is generalized, like falling into a pit, losing the HP is probably all there is to it. When the harm is specific, like an orc pulling your arm from its socket, HP should be part of the effect but not the entirety of it. The bigger issue is dealing with the newly busted arm: how do you swing a sword or cast a spell? Likewise having your head chopped off is not HP damage, it\u2019s just you being dead.
      )

      \n On the subject of a range of possible damage, from not-too-serious to incredibly-scary, another thing comes to mind:

      There are some attacks and some forms of damage, which, if they get you... you're not likely to be able to "recover" from. Things like being swallowed whole, sliced in half, or immersed in lava come to mind.

      I'd say that, in those situations, you can roll to resist the harm, but if you fail, you're done for. There's not likely going to be a chance to roll to recover later (depending on the details of the situation, of course - if your friends slay the beast and cut open its stomach, maybe they can resuscitate you!).

      \n
      My reading is that getting hit does NOT deal Hp damage- it has fictional consequences only. So the player thinks about how badly they wanna avoid this blow and then stakes some Hp on the roll to avoid it. Hit or miss, that Hp is gone. This is a really cool idea!... A character could theoretically always spend 0-1 Hp and rely on luck to survive, which seems fun and alright. Or they could strategically conserve Hp for the most fictionally dire hits coming their way. It is more complicated, overall, but it's a very neat idea.
      \nHP = Mistake Potential. Best analogy ever Eon. I have been using that wording for ages when we play, and mixing in a lot of fictional consequences, but your summation is spot on. I love it.

      \n
      When you get hurt, you can (and should) choose to try to resist the harm. You're a hero, after all:\n
      When your character suffers a horrible injury or wound , you may choose to resist harm. Roll+number of hit points you'd like to spend. One a hit, choose options. On a 13+, all of them. On a 10+, choose 2. On a 7-9, choose 1.
      * This injury doesn't hamper you, you can ignore it for now
      * You got lucky: you'll find later that the injury's not as big a deal as it seemed
      * You maintain your position or advantage, and can react immediately (if you do, take +1 forward)
      On a miss, whatever else the MC says happens, you suffer the injury in full, right now.
      \nThere's no minimum hit point expenditure: you may always choose to spend 0 hit points. However, the 13+ category here exists mainly as an incentive to spend at least one hit point every time you make this roll.

      When you've suffered any dangerous injuries and you have a moment to gather yourself, you can try to recover from them (with thanks to @Johnstone for the basic idea for this move):

      When you fight to recover from trauma, roll+number of hit points you'd like to spend. On a 10+, you recover quickly and as fully as possible under the circumstances. On a 7-9, you will recover but the MC chooses one:
      * It will take extra time or require specific conditions (e.g. bed rest, an antidote to a poison, smelling salts)
      * You need outside help (perhaps skilled medical care)
      * You find yourself in a tight spot or unpleasant situation
      * It marks you in a permanent way
      On a miss, whatever else the MC says happens, you do not recover.
      \n
      We tried out these last Friday. It was cool but if you have WoDu's amount of HP then it's quite lethal.
      Or, maybe I should be very strict about the "suffer a horrible insury" part. But how to systemize this condition?

      As for how many hit points you can regain and how often, that's a dial to play with. The more hit points you have, the less easy it should be to regain them. Maybe if all you've got is 1d6 hit points, you regain them all after each significant action scene, or whenever you rest.
      \nI would reroll those hit points each time you rest or have downtime... or perhaps at the moment when you go into battle, after a rest or break in the action, for extra suspense.

      \n Thank you for your hints. But what could reach up to a 'horrible injury or wound'? How would you systemize a light wound? For me, that's the hard question.

      Btw our main problem came from the fact that I mixed your idea with some of my own, but the synergy was missing:

      \n
      \n Well, I'm not sure this is a good system for a "80s beat 'em up game", but, then again, it could be, if you have a lot of hit points.

      The idea with a "light wound" is that it, by default:

      * Takes the wind out of you (you've lost the initiative, in other words, and you can't react)
      * Hampers you in the fight
      * Could cause trouble for you later

      The only difference is that we don't assume it can kill you.

      So, basically, any injury is potentially pretty bad news. Let's say someone's swinging a chair around and hits you in the head, not hard enough to break your skull or anything, but, you know, it hurts, and there's some blood.

      Unless you resist harm and roll well enough to shake off some of these effects, we might say that your character is knocked down or stunned momentarily (#1), is now having trouble finding his bearings, and therefore 'acting under fire', or rolling at a penalty, for the duration of the fight (#2), and afterwards the MC can also make some kind of move against him as a result of the injury (#3): maybe he's got a concussion and needs to go to the hospital or suffer some kind of long-term damage, or maybe he's just bleeding heavily and will pass out if he can't get it taken care over soon.

      That's the idea. If your hero rolls a 10+, she might choose:

      1. To win the fight at all costs:

      Accept the brain damage, bleeding, or other problems she might face later, but
      (1) choose to react immediately, and
      (2) ignore the injury for the duration of the fight.

      2. Try to win the fight in one dramatic moment:

      (1) She chooses to react immediately, and
      (2) not to deal with long-term consequences ("you got lucky").

      If she can't take out her opponent with her counter-attack, she'll be at a disadvantage for the rest of the fight, however.

      3. Try to keep herself out of harm's way:

      Accept getting the wind knocked out of her, but
      (1) choose to ignore the wound for now, and
      (2) classify it as non-serious ("you got lucky").

      This choice basically tells us that the blow knocked her off her feet, but otherwise didn't do any lasting damage at all.

      So, as you can see, most injuries under this system are potentially dangerous. It's the roll, rather, which tells us retroactively just how serious it was. Like Frodo getting "run through" with the Orc's spear: it turns out it wasn't too bad, after all. On the other hand, if our 80s game heroine took a blow to the head, chose the first option above, and then failed her "recover" roll after the fight, she could bleed to death or end up with a concussion.
      \n

      \n I like player facing moves. I used your AW harm hack for 16 sessions or so and I thought it worked great. But I sometimes I wonder if those moves and these DW moves cross the line as John Harper put it here: http://mightyatom.blogspot.com.au/2010/10/apocalypse-world-crossing-line.html

      My understanding of crossing the line is that with player moves bad stuff should be chosen by the GM. It's weak when one person initiates, resolves, and colors-in all by themselves. There's a reason the moves in the book "bounce back and forth" in terms of who says what.\ufeff

      Do you think this is a potential problem Paul?
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nMarch 2014 \n edited March 2014
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n That's a good question. I can see two reasons to see it as a problem:

      1. One is that the moves are not enough of a "back and forth" between the MC and the player.

      2. The other is that the player has too much say in something which their character wouldn't be able to control.

      I feel that the first one (1) works well, because the player move is always in response to an MC move. Something bad happens, and the player has to respond by making the move and making choices. Particularly in this case: the MC says, "the hammer comes down on your leg with the crack of a hundred thunderbolts, throwing sparks to each side", and the player responds by making the move. The MC will often have something to say in response, as well, since she needs to finish the narration or describe how the player is hampered (unless the player rolled a 13+, of course, which is a sort of critical hit here).

      The second is potentially an issue for some, and has been brought up before in these discussions. My feeling is that choosing bad things from a list is a fundamental part of AW's design, and works really well in this situation: sure, you get a lot of say in what's going on, but none of the choices are happy choices. I very much like how that works out in play.

      To me, this is how AW goes: the MC narrates some potentially bad stuff (an MC move), the player reacts, often by making a move (e.g. acting under fire), and then has a difficult choice to make (a hard bargain or ugly choice). That's the dynamic I wanted to create here with these various harm hacks.

      But you're the one I should be asking here: it sounds like you've run a full campaign using those rules, or something close to one. Did this issue come up for you? Can you describe a situation where it felt like it would have been better to have more MC input, or a different dynamic in play? What was the fictional situation, and how did it feel awkward?

      I'm glad to hear someone's getting some use out of all this, anyway! Thank you for letting me know, it's pleasant news.

I can't stop the blow from coming, but I still have a small amount of agency as to where/how it hits me.

      \n I'm late to the conversation, but I want to ask:

      What about clerics? It LOOKS like this using these moves could completely "fictionalize" their healing, especially if you are using the "Life Time Supply" version. Cure Light Wounds heals the scratch you got from the wolf's claws, but not the gash from the dragon.

      \n Good question, Josh.

      I've been thinking about that a bit myself. I've had a few different ideas:

      1. Get rid of clerics altogether. I find they're a bit of an odd D&D class: aside from dealing with undead and healing spells, their function isn't super clear. "What's a cleric? Do you sit at a desk and full out forms all day?"

      2. Treat healing spells as a "divine favour" thing, kind of like a powerup. Yeah, the healing spells now act kind of like a "Bless" spell, giving you better odds of surviving whatever's coming up.

      3. Casting healing on someone's who's wounded can give them HP to spend immediately on the "recovery" roll, which might be an easy and elegant way to handle this.

      4. Treat healing narratively. The character failed a resist harm or recover roll and has an arm that doesn't work/a wound that's bleeding out/an infected foot. The healing spell removes the problem.

      \n
      \n

      \n
      \n I'd like to know that, too.

      My only new thought about this whole thing has been (if you're playing Dungeon World):

      * In combat, think in terms of softer moves (an "attack" which disadvantages a PC, but doesn't threaten to kill them) and hard moves (a potentially deadly blow).

      On a 7-9 outcome which suggests damage (Hack & Slash, for example), use a softer move first. Then apply actual damage when it's been properly set up - the hero is in a tough spot and death is looming.

      For example, Gregor is fighting an ogre. They're on even ground (or Gregor has an advantage). He rolls a 7-9: he harms the ogre, but the monster also swings down its club, knocking him off his feet and into the wall. Now he's prone, and the ogre is looming over him, prepared to bring the club down on his puny form and finish him.

      NOW Gregor has been maneuvered into a position where he could potentially snuff it. NOW we can think in terms of actual hard moves if another opportunity to hurt him comes up.

      So, if the ogre is in position to kill him, has the upper hand - now if he decides to Hack & Slash again (instead of trying to get out of this dangerous situation), on a 7-9 feel free to have the ogre smash his head in (and roll the harm move).

      I think this should feel nicely dramatic and balance well for play.


      My current position on hit points and so forth:

      1. Draw one box (in pen) on your character sheet for every two hit points she has. If you have an odd number of hit points, add another box, but put one line through it (in pen).

      2. Armor has one box per point of armor, which operates the same way.

      Whenever you roll to resist harm, you can check off boxes for a bonus to the roll, to a maximum of +4. The first line through any box is made in pencil, and erased when the character rests and receives first aid. If you ever cross off a box a second time (making a second line through the box, like an "X"), it's permanent. Make the second line in pen.

      Armor boxes are cleared whenever you have a chance to rest, and even "permanent" lines can be restored by an armorer/blacksmith, so long as it seems believable that the armor can be saved.

      Hit point boxes are cleared at some rate based on your class's starting hit points (perhaps half your starting hit point bonus, per day), but permanent crosses cannot be removed.

      When you level up, add some new boxes. Maybe 1 for a Wizard, 2 for a Thief, 3 for a Cleric, and 4 for a Fighter.

      \n Been giving it a fair crack Paul during the last few sessions and the 'boxes' system is genius. We've made magical / inherent armour ablative too, requiring some fictional justification for their renewal (usually rest between adventures). +4 Max spend per roll is about right. Also the soft / hard GM 'harming' move paradigm. It lets me ignore damage dealt (by the monsters) and solely focus on their moves. The nice thing being you can easily envision a soft/hard alternate for each.


      One thing I've been mucking around with is giving the players their HP as little gem tokens and as they spend them to avoid harm (bonuses on rolls), they give them to me (the GM).
      This gives me a visual reminder of how much mistake potential (as a group) the party has spent on avoiding being hurt. But it also felt like it was then the potential for narrative 'threat' economy... What could I spend these HP on (since I'm not rolling dice)?
      Since I've been playing FAE with the kids, I almost subconsciously offered a HP token during a game as a compel when the Barbarian was sharing a meal with the party's tentative orcish allies.

      You have Herculean Appetites yeah? Here's a HP token back if you eat all that juicy, unknown meat sitting in front of you. You will impress the Orcs, but the gods know what creature you have just devoured.

      This worked a treat. It is effectively using the move offer an opportunity with a cost, but with a HP carrot. It also means that the players are aware that I can compel their playbook moves, bonds, alignment moves, racial moves, tags etc to make the story more complicated. The evocative names of their moves aren't just narratively interesting anymore, they can send the story into unexpected directions. Sorta like BW traits.

      \n That's pretty cool, Noofy.

      The only problems:

      1. That first die is going to be a lot more effective than a +1 (not necessarily a problem; in fact, this way there's no need to cap how many you spend per roll, since their effectiveness falls off the more you use).

      2. You can never achieve the "critical hit" 13+ result, which I would find a little disappointing. I added it in not just for the dramatic value, but also to encourage people to actually spend at least one hit point per roll.

      Maybe rolling three 6's gives you the 13+ result? Still makes it damn rare, though.
      \n

      Whaaa? As I elucidated a while ago, we have been using dice pools in DW for a while and taking best/worst of instead of a flat bonus/penalty.
      \nI fooled around with this a bunch earlier on the AW forums: Alternate dice

      \n Paul, I mashed up this DW damage hack with your AW harm hack. I wanted to keep using Monster damage dice . I haven't tried it yet but I thought you might be interested nonetheless.

      When you take damage, roll your damage dice + number of hit points or armour you\u2019d like to spend. Then the GM rolls your attacker's damage and calculates its total as normal. If your result is higher, mark 1. Otherwise, mark 1 and the GM marks 1. If your result is half the GM's or less, the GM marks another 1.
      - you lose track or miss noticing something important (GM decides what)
      - you lose your footing or position
      - you lose your grip on whatever you're holding
      - you're stunned or dazed so can't react for a few moments
      - you're pinned or trapped; defy danger to shake it off
      - you're a mess: bruised, swollen, bleeding; roll for recovery after fight.
      If you're already a mess, then the options below may be marked:
      - bleeding heavily: -2 to recovery roll
      - wounds won't heal: when recovering GM always chooses one complication in addition to anything else.
      - a limb is broken, maimed, cut off or disabled.
      Only mark unmarked options. Erase a mark when you've dealt with its effect.
      \n

      \n Hey!

      I don't know if WoDu is still hype those days, but I for one really love its concepts, even though I only ran it using group text conversations on Skype for some reason. Anyway, I'm going to run World of Shadows or some kind of Mohawk & Mirrorshades old school Cyberpunk game soon, and the Harm mechanic that Paul_T suggested helped me a lot dealing with the damage and hit points part, that I had trouble swallowing last time I ran the game, so thanks for that!

      I had a question : if using this mechanic, how do NPCs work? AW-style, vanilla WoDu, or did someone come up with something else in that regard? Right now, I think I'd go with AW-style harm for NPCs, essentially because players really dig the Anyone Can Die trope that goes on in my games, thanks AW again for that.

      Actually, since I'm poking a dead thread with a stick and I'm not sure that's wise, I'll try to contribute with something!

      For online, text-based games, I heavily recommend the use of Rolz.org, which is as non-distracting as it is easy to use for the sake of rolling dices that everyone can see (not as a matter of trust, but more in order to try to get back some of what disappears in session that don't happen face-to-face). Hexographer is a free-ish software for easy hex carthographing, which is really cool for wilderness adventures or just having-a-map when you happen to be unable to draw. Myself, I don't think I did anything really new to add to the discussion, sadly.

      On an half-related note, I'm currently doing an experiment to help me get better at structured improvisation (structured as opposed to on the fly improvisation which fueled most of my GMing life before, with sometimes great, sometimes less great success) : using the WoDu basics, I'm trying to design a setting using only mechanics to do so. Everything I write or say about the setting and game must be either through mechanics (like, one of the stat is called Sugar, it is used when the surnatural or the wireless net is involved, and is also the name of the drug people must take if they wish to keep interacting with spirits or surviving being connected to the net), or stated during my turn as the MC (when narrating, which here consists in being the perceptions of the characters, meaning I don't explain things more than the characters' would need to). Of course, the monsters' moves from Dungeon World is a great help to that effect, but as are old timey Random Tables that are the perfect excuse to populate various landscapes, or determine which kind of landscapes can be found in this world.

      I started doing that when I realized how hard it was for me to GM Powered by the Apocalypse games with players that aren't familiar with "story games", since it actually provides great tool for narration, but not anything that could be used to be lazy (like, for exemple, a combat system that stays mostly out of the fiction).

      Which brings another question in order to this not be a simple comment-question thing : does WoDu (and maybe PbtA games in general) give you more or less work as a MC/GM than a "trad" game? Did it help you improve in some way your gaming life, and if yes in what way?

      Voil\xe0 !

      EDIT : also, please forgive my many mistakes at it is currently 7AM where I live and I haven't slept tonight for reasons, this plus not speaking english regularly makes me prompt to terrible mistakes. Sorry in advance.

      \n Tana\xebl,

      You raise some interesting and important questions, but they are far beyond the scope of this thread. I'd encourage you to start a new discussion about a large question like "Do PbtA games give you more or less work as a GM than a 'trad' game"? In this thread, it will just be lost.

      I'd like to hear how playing with my damage hack worked out for you, however.

      When I play AW, I use AW-style harm for NPCs (purely descriptive). With Dungeon World or WoDu, I'd probably use standard hit points, however, since the variable output of damaga for the characters matters.

      \n I have to say, I'm a bit (lot) intimidated by the forum, which is why I tend to not create new discussions, since I don't think I'd be able to keep up with the discussions because of my limited capability to express my thinking in english, among other things.

      I think I'll run World of Shadows with your damage hack saturday, and maybe post the play report here, if that would be more in focus with the thread's subject?\n
      When I play AW, I use AW-style harm for NPCs (purely descriptive). With Dungeon World or WoDu, I'd probably use standard hit points, however, since the variable output of damaga for the characters matters.
      \nSo you have no problem with dealing with damages as fiction for the PCs point of view while keeping the "still not dead" abstract approach of HP for NPCs and monsters? Makes me wonder about the principle that says PCs are the center of the world in AW (which IMO isn't quite true in my WoDu which I try to play more like a "trad" game).

      The only thing that bugs me with this is the fact that I'm not sure it helps players to actually stay in "narrative" mode when fighting : if the result is description+technical damage whether they explain how they try to kill the goblin or just plain say "I hit the goblin". On the other hand, it CAN be very taxing to come up with tons of different way to basically say you're murdering someone/something.

      \n You really shouldn't be afraid of anyone here! Your contributions are very welcome. Start a new thread - you can use the exact same post, if you like - and I'm sure you'll receive some good replies. Story Games is a friendly place! (If you'd prefer, I could start the thread for you - your questions here are interesting ones, and deserve a wider audience.)

      Besides, it is far better to start a new topic of conversation than to try to pull an old one in a new direction (which could be frustrating to some readers).

      Meanwhile, I'd love to hear about your game. My recommendation is to use the WoDu rules as written, but use my hit point hack with the following additional notes:

      1. Every time a character can rest, apply first aid, and have some food, they can reroll their hit points.

      2. Save wounding for "hard moves", as I describe above. On a typical "7-9" result, put the characters in a tough spot first, and only then use a hard move to try to kill them. You don't want people dying every time they roll a 7-9, after all. Think soft moves on these instead - make the situation more complicated for them, in other words. Save getting stabbed to death for a moment where they clearly have it coming.

      In answer to your question about hit points, I don't mind keeping it for NPCs/monsters because it's simple and doesn't take up any "screen time". I want PC injuries to be dealt with in detail, but monster injuries aren't that important, you know? Roll a die and subtract the hit points... seems like a simple enough abstraction that we can then move on with the game. After all, we really just need to know: is the monster dead yet? Hit points answer that question very nicely.

      You can use a different approach, of course. But keep in mind that WoDu depends at least a little on various characters being better at hurting things (e.g. the Slay ability, various weapons, etc). If you don't have hit points for monsters, then these abilities no longer mean anything.

      (Not that it's impossible to come up with a different way to make these things matter; it's just that keeping hit points is the *simplest* way to do it, without starting to hack the game even more. If you do come up with some clever method, post about it here - I'd love to hear it. One of my favourites is to use the 2d6+adds roll itself as a "damage roll"... but that's another conversation for later.)
      \n

      \n

      The only thing that bugs me with this is the fact that I'm not sure it helps players to actually stay in "narrative" mode when fighting : if the result is description+technical damage whether they explain how they try to kill the goblin or just plain say "I hit the goblin". On the other hand, it CAN be very taxing to come up with tons of different way to basically say you're murdering someone/something.
      \nBy the way, the way to keep the narrative moving in a fight is to make sure that the fictional situation is transformed after each roll/move (keep in mind some moves do not require a roll, but are just part of the MC-Player conversation).

      It's really not much fun - as you point out - if you just have two figures stand in front of each other on an abstract board and roll dice to hit each other.

      For instance, if Bobby the Warrior is in combat with some goblins, here are possible outcomes:

      10+ - Bobby does extremely well, and things shift in his advantage. Maybe his opponents are slain, or they flee/retreat. Or perhaps they switch to more desperate tactics. He gains ground, they fall back to a different position and now they're trying to bar the door against him. That kind of thing.

      6 or less - Bobby is screwed. A goblin hits him from the side while another trips him. He's lying on the ground bleeding and his sword has been knocked out of his hand.

      7-9 - A combination of the two. He has struck his enemies, but he's also in trouble. Maybe he killed one of the goblins, but now the others have him surrounded, pointing crossbows at him from a safe distance. Or are holding his companion with a knife to his throat. Or he has pushed through to the next room, only to find himself face to face with a giant toad.

      You can see that, in any of those situations, there shouldn't be any difficulty for Bobby's player to "come up with an exciting narration" - he's not even worried about that, he's just trying to figure out what the hell he is going to do next.

      All hit points do is tell you whether a monster has been killed or not; it's a very minor point.
      \n

      \n In my hack, i only use 4 attributes -STR DEX CHA WIS- and i'm making an alternative system for monster damage:

      Players don't have HP
      Monsters roll their damage die in this table (after applying armor for each die rolled)

      1: Armor breaks (-1 to your armor)
      2: Beaten (-1 STR)
      3: Shaken (-1 DEX)
      4: Scared (-1 CHA)
      5: Shocked (-1 WIS)
      6: Wounded (-1 to all rolls)
      7 or more: Wasted.

      If you get the same state twice, you get Wounded instead
      If you get Wounded twice, you're Wasted.
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \nI like the resting rule, reminds me of Barbarians of Lemuria, which is awesome because even though innjury cummulates, you can still Not Die after a time, preventing the players to be afraid of, say, continuing the dungeon instead of retreating into town each time someone is downed.

      I could look out for the text base game if you want, from what I remember, the meaty stuff was : while I didn't let enough time pass between actions (not second-by-second during combat, but mostly one "action", old reflexe from D&D), the fighting was really intense due to the high lethality (four players, eight characters, three survived after a three days expedition in the forest to save the village from orcs, a simple plot I used to make the characters think about they're relationships between themselves and the other villagers, since they totally belonged instead of being "adventurers", making them somewhat a sacrifice more than heroes. The survivors left the village after perceiving a reward from the mayor.

      The magic rules I didn't understand at the time, but thanks to reading a lot of stuff here (Dungeon Girls, yay!), I think I can handle it now. At least I love making up new spirits and rituals on the fly.

      \nThat sounds reasonable. When I played Warhammer 2E, the book said that everyone, characters and NPCs/Monsters alike where subject to that rule where you don't die unless you get to 0HP then suffer a sufficiently crippling injury to eventually stop moving. This meant very, very long combat with tons of bookkeeping, even tho it sounded awesome in theory. I quickly learned that most of the people I knew GMed Warhammer with a houserule of "when it's not a PC, it dies at 0 Wounds".

      I wanted to quote the exemple on various results in combat but I seem to have lost it. (which makes me think that the flow of time could be an interesting point to rise for the PbtA thread).\n

      1: Armor breaks (-1 to your armor)
      2: Beaten (-1 STR)

      \n I may have missed it, but if you want to have some monsters apply "more damage" than others you simply give them a lethality rating of 1-3(5?)

      \n Big_J_Money, that suggestion was already made earlier in the thread.

      I would encourage you to try these rules as written. I feel that adjusting the ratio of hit points to the roll, while not a terrible idea, goes against the spirit of a game like Dungeon World. For instance, the Wizard and Thief are already "more squishy": they have fewer hit points and they wear less armour.

      More importantly, the whole point of this hack is to take the focus away from the quantitative aspects of RPG combat and to bring it to the qualitative aspects. Instead of judging wounds based on the number of hit points they inflict (which is what you are trying to bring back in with this change), you should judge them based on their description, location, and so forth.

      Do you really think that a player who is hit by a goblin's whip across his back while trying to swim across the river is going to spend the same number of hit points on the roll as someone who encounters this (below)?\n
      "The creature's jaws close around your torso and bear down, rotten teeth the size of kitchen knives piercing your corselet with a sickening crunch."
      \nThat said, if you try it, let us know how it went (I'm always open to changing my mind)!

      \n Fair enough. I'm not actually playing DW/WoD at the moment, but this is interesting conversation nonetheless.

      Another possible variant for people who like to geek out over medieval combat? "Hit points" actually represnt defensive combat skills, which each have ratings. Learning dodge and/or parry gives you 3 more points, riposte gives you 5, etc. I'm going to use that notion, I think.

      Edit: So for the curious, how I'm implementing this is to allow characters to exhaust (temporarily sacrifice) combat abilities in order to increase their chance of avoiding an injury during a wounding roll. Thanks for the discussion, this turned out to be very helpful for me. It allowed me to get rid of a somewhat clunky "shock meter" mechanic I was only somewhat interested in.

      \n@Paul_T
      Playing without HP - can't remember if I answered this already.

      We removed HPs completely and pushed all the damage into the fiction. Since that's kinda what WoDu is pushing for anyway. So damage becomes what you get hit with based on the fictional setup.

      So when battle a stone golem with a giant stone hammer the fictional outcome is being hit with 200 lbs of stone is pretty straight forward. As I usually Announce some Future Badassness before hitting folks hard. We use post it notes to track how bad things are. Like if your hip got clipped by the massive hammer you are probably still alive but not doing so hot.

      We also thought about the When you Endure Harm move from Sagas.

That's also a nice and simple way to do it.

      I used another simplified take in The Silver Dragon's Tear.

      \n
      \n
      \n Here's a simple thought on how to handle deadly injuries with these rules, if desired:

      Every monster has a "Deadly" rating, from 1 (not deadly at all) to 10 (very deadly).

      When you roll to resist harm, if your total doesn't beat the monster's Deadly rating, the injury is life-threatening. You can still survive if you choose "the wound is not as bad as it seemed" or have an opportunity to roll to recover. (If you take an arrow through the chest and you get to a healer who can treat you, you get a roll. If the dragon bites you in half, you probably don't - especially if it swallows.)

      There we go. Finally a simple way to make death objective, if necessary, without any extra rolling or complications.

      What if, you take damage, and it is described as you've suggested, but the fictional consequences is there, and it has happened. THEN, you spend a resource to roll to reverse time to just before the injury, to instead avoid it. Like, basically a quick "rewind". Hmm.

      \n I'm working on a system to remove HP for good.
      There are three kinds of weapons:
      Raw weapons (fists,whips,slings) do no real damage. You can stun, entangle or hurt someone with them, but to do real damage with them you need to get a good position narratively.
      Serious weapons (swords, axes, bows,a tiger's fangs) deal 1 wound on a hit. Most enemies have a single hit.
      Big weapons (two handed weapons mostly, or a troll punch) deal 2 wounds.

      Weapons deal this damage on a hit. On a 7-9 you might get a choice or complication as always, on a 12+ maybe you can deal an extra wound or something.

      NPCS and PCs alike get armor dice based on their armor:
      light: 1 die
      heavy: 2 dice
      shield: +1 die
      certain foes can have +1 armor die representing a great battle prowess or resilience

      When an NPC or PC is hit, they roll all their armor dice. For each dice that comes up a 4,5 or 6, they prevent a wound. For each dice that comes up a 6, that piece of armor is broken and you cannot re-roll it until you repair it somehow.

      When an NPC reaches it's wound limit, it's dead or out of action (If it makes sense the GM may rule that it's just defeated or something if that's the tone of the game)

      PCs have a wound too by default. When they reach 0, they can roll+STR to stay on the fight. On a hit, they do despite all aesthetical wounds. On a 7-9, they suffer a debility (-1 to a stat)

      Any thoughts on this???? Am I forgetting anything?

      \n That sounds workable, although I don't love it.

      (Have you seen all the other damage hacks we've floated around? This is the only one that uses hit points, and it was designed specifically with that in mind - "Hmmm, let me find a use for hit points...")

      The main issue I could see is that armour effectively nullifies the effect of attacks a lot of the time. This could lead to a lot of rolls which do not DO anything.

      For instance, I'm fighting with my opponent. We both have heavy armour and serious weapons. (Or maybe my opponent has light armour and a shield.)

      Each time an attack lands, there is only a 25% chance of it actually hurting the other person. This would get far worse with more armour/battle prowess/resilience. That means that only 1/4 (successful!) attacks actually have a meaningful impact.

      \n Thanks for the feedback! Yeah, I read all the damage hacks,but I still wanted to try this new one.

      As for the slow rate of attacks, keep in mind that 25% chance of an attack landing is not a very low one, mind that a very single blow is potentially lethal. Armor will often be the only thing that prevents combats to finish in a single blow. Also having heavy armour you have a 11 in 36 chance of getting an armorbreaking/shieldbreaking hit, everytime you're hit. Funnily enough, as armor dice are all roll at once, the more armor you have, the more quickly it is gonna break too.

      "That means that only 1/4 (successful!) attacks actually have a meaningful impact."

      What I mean is that breaking the armor of the other guy is part of that impact too.


      The game I have in mind deals a lot with resource management and I want armor repairing to be a thing.

      Also you deal an extra wound on a 12, which is not very hard to achieve (1 in 6 if you have a +2 modifier. Weapon prowess skill also adds +1 to the roll)

      My main issue with this system is that I don't know how to picture the scenario where you hit your opponent and only wound him, but he's still on the fight. Maybe if you hit on a 7-9 you may choose to gain an advantage over him (wound him, grapple him or whatever is fit) without really dealing a lethal blow. Thats why I added the "resilience"die: to portray that kind of enemies that can endure a sword cut just because. When you roll a 6 on it, you lose you battling confidence.

      Also, as all armor dice are roll together, In case you roll sixes, the order of armor breaking is always: 1.shield 2.armor 3.resilience

      \n That's a good point about the armour damage factoring in! Especially if you can "lose" your battle prowess or resilience.

      Still, I think it's a problem that a lot of successful combat actions will carry no *fictional* ramifications. In the same way that knocking off 10 hit points is boring, knocking off a die of "resilience" is a boring outcome. More problematically, though, hit point loss occurs pretty much on every successful attack, which makes it feel like progress, whereas in your system you could have a lot of attacks have no effect at all. If 1/3 of attacks damage armour and 25% actually hurt someone, that means half of your attacks do *nothing* at all. And those are *successful* attacks! (Unless you're going to use the damage roll to replace any kind of combat/hit/dodge rolls, then it's better but still pretty uneventful.)
      \n

      \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
