      \n\nJune 2016 \n edited June 2016
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n For comparison, you'll notice that in my hack EVERY time an attack lands, there is a fictional effect and a mechanical effect. We've already described an injury - there's impact, blood, etc, which can affect what you do next (e.g. you're bleeding, and that could leave a trail). But then we roll to see how bad a problem it's going to be going forward. Even on a 13+ roll (the least 'interesting') we have a sense that the character got lucky and the system tells that they can 'react immediately' and take +1 forward, which is something we can use to move play forward, and adds to our imagination of the situation. We know that the character can barrel forward through the pain and that they now effectively "have the initiative", plus an incentive to strike back or do some dramatic.

      Your idea here is more like a traditional "roll for damage" system, where (in my experience) a roll of "0 damage" gives absolutely nothing to go on. It doesn't drive play forward, and we don't really know what has meaningfully happened. ("Uh, ok. So, who's next?")

      Just my opinion, of course, but it's precisely what I came up with this hack to avoid in the first place.

      \n Not every foe has armor. Most enemies have one (mooks) or none armor dice. I don't believe that some null hits are a problem. In fact my first idea was:

      2d6+STR-enemy armor ---> hit on a 10, cost on 7-9

      but that made combat too fast. Most of the time you hit, even if there were consequences. The only way to make enemies not insta-death was using some sort of wound system (HP or other) Is not bad either, but I like the sense of uncertainity that armor dice give.

      \n OK, so I realize that this thread is over 2 years but I've been doing a lot of math on point spend systems lately and I can contribute with some numbers here.
      I've used this to analyze Gumshoe and Numenera.

      Now, I looked through the whole thread but it was hard to find a current version of the hack proposed in the thread.

      So, here's the deal: to show what I mean, I'll do the math on the system proposed in Paul's first post in the thread. And if you guys want to know how it would apply to the current, Paul, please post the system as it stands after all the revisions proposed in the thread that you've accepted.

      The basic concept behind my analysis is something Aviatrix come up with: above all, you want to avoid wasting points because you underspent. I started thinking "Wasting points because you overspend is just as bad" -- but that just lead me to "OK, then spending 0 is always best". Obviously failure has its own cost, but... can you quantify that cost? Turns out yes.
      I came up with the concept of "weight of failure" which is "how many points lost would failing the roll be as painful as?" And then, as you like to say, "ran the numbers" and then turned the table on its head and came up with this (here's the version for this hack):\n
      ((7
        ((0 ((0 0)))
         (7 ((1 2.81) (0 2.92)))
         (8 ((2 2.97) (1 3.08) (0 3.33)))
         (10 ((3 3.22) (2 3.31) (1 3.64) (0 4.17)))
         (15 ((4 3.58) (3 3.64) (2 4.14) (1 5.03) (0 6.25)))
         (32 ((5 4.03) (4 4.06) (3 5.06) (2 6.97) (1 9.75) (0 13.33)))))
       (10
        ((0 ((0 0)))
         (5 ((5 3.83) (4 3.94) (0 4.17)))
         (6 ((5 4) (4 4.22) (3 4.61) (0 5)))
         (7 ((6 4.08) (5 4.17) (4 4.5) (3 5.03) (2 5.69) (0 5.83)))
         (9 ((6 4.25) (5 4.5) (4 5.06) (3 5.86) (2 6.86) (1 7.39) (0 7.5)))
         (12 ((7 4.44) (6 4.5) (5 5) (4 5.89) (3 7.11) (2 8.61) (1 9.56) (0 10)))
         (29 ((8 4.89) (7 4.92) (6 5.92) (5 7.83) (4 10.61) (3 14.19) (2 18.53) (1 21.83) (0 24.17)))))
       (13
        ((0 ((0 0)))
         (5 ((9 4.28) (8 4.44) (7 4.89) (0 5)))
         (6 ((9 4.36) (8 4.61) (7 5.17) (6 5.97) (0 6)))
         (7 ((9 4.44) (8 4.78) (7 5.44) (6 6.39) (0 7)))
         (9 ((10 4.56) (9 4.61) (8 5.11) (7 6) (6 7.22) (5 8.72) (0 9)))
         (10 ((10 4.58) (9 4.69) (8 5.28) (7 6.28) (6 7.64) (5 9.31) (0 10)))
         (12 ((10 4.64) (9 4.86) (8 5.61) (7 6.83) (6 8.47) (5 10.47) (4 11.83) (0 12)))
         (16 ((10 4.75) (9 5.19) (8 6.28) (7 7.94) (6 10.14) (5 12.81) (4 14.72) (3 15.94) (0 16)))
         (17 ((10 4.78) (9 5.28) (8 6.44) (7 8.22) (6 10.56) (5 13.39) (4 15.44) (3 16.78) (0 17)))
         (23 ((10 4.94) (9 5.78) (8 7.44) (7 9.89) (6 13.06) (5 16.89) (4 19.78) (3 21.78) (2 22.94) (0 23)))
         (26 ((11 5) (10 5.03) (9 6.03) (8 7.94) (7 10.72) (6 14.31) (5 18.64) (4 21.94) (3 24.28) (2 25.69) (0 26)))
         (36 ((11 5) (10 5.31) (9 6.86) (8 9.61) (7 13.5) (6 18.47) (5 24.47) (4 29.17) (3 32.61) (2 34.86) (1 35.97) (0 36))))))
      \nThis works like this. The outermost numbers, 7, 10, 13 -- that's the difficulty you want. The next layer in is the salient weights. For example, for difficulty 10, these are 0, 5, 6, 7, 9, 12 and 29. Any other weight, say weight 36 or weight 1000, they have the same math as the weight under it. So if the weight is 15, look at the 12 line. This also helps make it possible to even estimate the given weight. It's tricky to determine "Does this hurt as bad as losing 15 points, or as losing 16 points?" But to make the judgement call whether it hurts as bad as 12 or as bad as 29 is probably more doable.

      Then the next layer in, let's say we're using difficulty 10, weight 9, that's a list of pairs. The left number in each pair is the amount of point you should spend if you want to minimize waste, and the right number in each pair is the average waste over 2d6 given that point spend and that weight.

      In the list of pairs, you should spend the leftmost number if at all possible, and failing that, go right. For example, in our example of desiring a 10+, guesstimating the failure about as bad as the thought of losing 9-11 points, we should spend 6 points if at all posibble. If that's not possible, we should spend 5 points, but with the awareness that the average waste will go up from 4.24 to 4.5. Even if our points are precious, if we only have six points, it's better to spend them them than to spend 5 because we will lose on average fewer points.

      Of course, in a situation where points are precious, "weights" are hopefully lower, too.

      Now, these long "priority" lists (the innermost lists, of pairs) were added when I analyzed Numenera since in it spending is so expensive that you seldom can spend enough to make it a guaranteed success. In Gumshoe you have such an abundance of points that you can cut the lists down to just the leftmost entry (and then collapse duplicates, such as in this example for difficulty 10? In both weight 6 and weight 5 you want to spend 5 points if at all possible, so you can remove the line with weight 6.

      Doing this simplification (which, again, only is doable for a game where you typically do have enough to spend to bring the difficulty down to zero), we get this version:\n
      (7
       ((0 (0 0))
        (7 (1 2.81))
        (8 (2 2.97))
        (10 (3 3.22))
        (15 (4 3.58))
        (32 (5 4.03))))
      (10
       ((0 (0 0))
        (5 (5 3.83))
        (7 (6 4.08))
        (12 (7 4.44))
        (29 (8 4.89))))
      (13
       ((0 (0 0))
        (5 (9 4.28))
        (9 (10 4.56))
        (26 (11 5))))
      \nAgain, this is a subset of the numbers in the previous table, just that if you do have a whole bunch of points, these are the only thresholds that matter.

      Now, this is actually not a super obvious thing to bring over from the binary worlds of Gumshoe and Numenera. It's not like you decide before you roll that "yes, please, I'd like a 10 or higher".
      Rather, the three thresholds (two typically, three in the 13+ version) have three different weights (obv just use 0 for 13+ if 10+ gives the same benefits).
      So I went back to the drawing board and I came up with:\n
      (((1 1 1) (0 1))
       ((3 2 1) (5 2))
       ((3 2 2) (6 2.33))
       ((4 4 3) (7 2.78))
       ((6 6 6) (8 3.5))
       ((10 10 10) (9 4.08))
       ((16 16 16) (10 4.53))
       ((34 34 34) (11 5)))
      \nSo the the trio is the weights for complete failure, 7-9, or 10-12. And then the pair is the points you "should" spend, and the average waste for that spend given those three weights.

      If you never care about the 13+ result, you get this:\n
      (((1 1 0) (0 0.83))
       ((2 2 0) (5 1.58))
       ((4 4 0) (6 1.83))
       ((8 8 0) (7 2.14))
       ((22 22 0) (8 2.5)))
      \nTL;DR: Spending everything in order to completely guarantee success is typically a Good Idea.
      Any questions?\n
      HP = Mistake Potential. Best analogy ever Eon. I have been using that wording for ages when we play, and mixing in a lot of fictional consequences, but your summation is spot on. I love it.
      \nYeah, this is pretty good! In vanilla D&D I use HP like that. As the text says: "Hit points represent a combination of physical and mental durability, the will to live, and luck". I use it for composure, footing, hope, luck, fatigue...

      When you get to zero HP, you roll for a serious wound. We've had people walking around with broken ribs, with crutches because a fireball took their foot etc.
      As long as you have over zero HP, you can get your composure back by resting for a day or so. (As per RAW.)

      My biggest issue with the original hack in the first post (and if the thread has moved on, I apologize) is that it to me seems like it would feel like a retcon. You got hurt! But then it wasn't so bad.
      In my mind when arcane blasts and arrows are hailing upon the party (in vanilla D&D) it's like they get tired for dodging it. A bit silly but... no retcon at least. I dunno.

      \n And yes, this does mean that I generally think these spend points to add to your roll systems like Gumshoe and Numenera are fundamentally and inherently broken and bad, which surprised me because I never suspected that until Aviatrix pointed out that it's so very often best to spend exactly what you need to ensure 100% chance at success.
      Not sure that this HP spending system is in that category because I haven't understood the final version.

      \n Hey, instead of mistake potential (and going OT for a bit, back to vanilla D&D): "HP means Hit Points. When you have zero Hit Points, you get hit for real."

      Then (in 5e) you do death saves, and if you survive those, you, well, the lingering injuries thing is just a variant in the DMG so who knows what happens typically but not good! (The lingering injuries, they suggest to either use it every crit (way too much for me), or every time they go to zero (sweet spot for me), or every time they roll under six on a death save (too random for me). Or not use it at all, it's an optional rule after all. The risk of dying and 1d4 hours unconscious is bad enough to be the default. But I like it, it gives some flavor meaning to HP being Hit Points.

      \n (I've always had a bit of a problem with hit points for this reason - if losing hit points can mean that you didn't get hit in the first place, then why did we just bother with rolling to hit and armor class and all that stuff? If that's the interpretation, I'd prefer the Into the Odd solution where there is not "to-hit", and we just roll damage every turn.)

      The way I envision this going, there is no "retcon". You got hit and you got injured. Making a successful "resist harm" roll just means you're so badass that you clench your teeth and you keep going despite the pain (and you'll recover without complications, later, if you scored that option as well). Like an "action movie" injury. But the injury still happened; you're still dripping blood on the floor, there's still a hole in your spacesuit, you could still get infected, or whatever other consequences seem appropriate. There's no "retcon".

      Sandra,

      Fascinating! I'm not sure I follow the math, however. Can you give an example, and explain a little further why you think that this kind of system is inherently broken?
      \nGladly!
      Let's say you want to spend some HP to add to a 2d6 roll.
      What result can you live with? Let's say you can live with a 7-9. You should then spend 5.

      Let's say you really need a 10-12. You should then spend 7. (The 1 in 36 chance of rolling a 2 is so rare that if you do spend 8, you're so likely to overspend).

      Let's say you really need a 10-12. Like, getting 9 or lower will feel as bad as losing 22 HP or more! Then spend 8. This "emotional pain of losing 22" is mathematically calculated. If it only hurts as bad as losing 21, then keep spending 7.

      Let's say you do need a 13 or more. For 13, there are a couple of different pain thresholds. The table (the second to last table) has them all, so I'll just recount the top end here.
      If getting 12 or lower hurts as bad as losing 10 - 15 HP, spend 9. If it hurts as bad as losing 16 - 33 HP, spend 10. And only if it hurts as bad as losing 34 or more HP, spend the full 11.

      TL;DR: You should see a lot of 7 and 5 spends.

      Hence the brokenness of the system: the roll stops mattering and every hit just becomes a tax of 7 (or 5) HP. It becomes an overly cumbersome and time consuming (albeit very flavorful) way to say "You got hit, you lose 7 HP".\n
      I'm not *sure* if you were assuming this or not, but note that in the proposed hack, you always lose the points, regardless of what you roll. I think you got that, but just to be sure...
      \nThese sort of clarifications are exactly what I need. In this case, great, I assumed correctly that you do always lose the points.

      I've always had a bit of a problem with hit points for this reason - if losing hit points can mean that you didn't get hit in the first place, then why did we just bother with rolling to hit and armor class and all that stuff? If that's the interpretation, I'd prefer the Into the Odd solution where there is not "to-hit", and we just roll damage every turn.
      \nIf you can out-AC the hit, you don't even get tired, because you're so good at fending them off! And if they do out-hit your AC, it's oh, shit, you're all of a sudden that much closer to getting hit for real -- scary+exhausting!

      (It is inherently wonky since it started as a game about boats shooting each other and went through a more gruesome version where "one roll" represented a whole minute of fighting and HP most certainly were real wounds but the mechanic is fast and tense and so I keep it even though this interpretion is kinda ahistorical compared to the olden days, even though it certainly has support in the RAW.)\n
      The way I envision this going, there is no "retcon". You got hit and you got injured. Making a successful "resist harm" roll just means you're so badass that you clench your teeth and you keep going despite the pain (and you'll recover without complications, later, if you scored that option as well). Like an "action movie" injury. But the injury still happened; you're still dripping blood on the floor, there's still a hole in your spacesuit, you could still get infected, or whatever other consequences seem appropriate. There's no "retcon".
      \nI just have a hard time grokking action movie injuries (I don't watch a lot of action movies) and I have a hard time reconciling blood-dripping-on-the-floor with shrugging it of and resting it away after a day or so (which is a 4e/5e-ism, I'm sure, but in our B/X game we got clericed for free in the village every day anyway, they were generous with their CLW).

      Edit: I guess by "retcon", I mean the Fate-like "Oh, I guess it wasn't so bad after all"

      \n Couldn't resist quickly running through the numbers if you only care about getting a 7+, in which case:
      (((1 0 0) (0 0.42)) ((3 0 0) (4 1.22)) ((5 0 0) (5 1.25)))
      I.e if the weight is 0-2, spend 0. If the weight is 3-4, spend 4. If the weight is 5+, spend 5. So a lot of either spending 0 or 5 or emptying your pool completely.

      \n So if you like the blood-dripping but you're bad-ass... you can wrap that description around vanilla D&D's hp system, too, right? You lose some HP, blood drips on floor, and you clench teeth. If you have no more HP to lose, then you go down and get a real injury.

      This is the sorta assumption I think a lot of folks play with. Too many nicks and scratches for my taste. Like "I take a full ass fireball in the head. OK, I get a little sooty but I clench my teeth and soldier on" (having lost the 21 HP). Whereas in Sandraland it's more "OMG a full fireball headed my way! That could've really toasted me, I'm completely losing my composure here!" (having lost the 21 HP).

      \n I see what you're getting at! How meaningful is it to measure the "pain" of a broken arm in terms of hit points loss, though? Is there any good way to quantify that?

      You're right about many (most?) hits going for a "maximum" effective spend, in theory. With this hack, I would limit hit point expenditure to 4 per roll. Characters should have fairly few hit points to start with, as well.

      Ideally, the would use something other than a linear add, so it's more of a gamble, though.

      However, there are also a few more wrinkles (which I hope make this work even with linear +1's):

      1. You would typically expect a "roll to resist" AND a "roll to recover", so you have to consider whether you'd like to save points for *after the fight*, as well.

      2. Some portion of your hit points is "permanently used"; gone for good if you spend them (with Dungeon World, it would be half of them - but that rule is buried in the thread, so I don't blame you if you missed it). That should make the choice a little more involved, as well.

      3. You have to judge the severity of the current wound relative to a possible *later* wound. If a nasty dragon thing scratches your arm, do you want to use the hit points now? What if it breathes fire on you as its next move, and you've spent all of them?

      I don't know if it's enough, having said all that.

      As for "action movie injuries" - do you visualize all D&D fights as a string of misses, then?

      If so, wouldn't it be more logical to increase AC with levels instead of HP? And assign damage ratings based on how easy something is to avoid? For instance, a swarm of insects should do more "damage" than a 2-ton boulder thrown from a great height.

      It seems to me that fantasy novels and such have just as strong a tradition of "scary-sounding injury that the character can pretty much ignore until later" as action movies.

      I do agree that it wouldn't work all that well in a D&D-esque game where lots of "blows" are landed in the course of a fight. Much better something AW-or-WoD-esque, where an injury is a fairly rare and significant thing*. (And you can afford to maybe get hit once before you go down for good.)


      *: AW's armour rules actually make injury far too easy to shrug off, which bugs me as well! But the design intent - you can take one bullet "for free", but the next could kill you - is in line with my preferences for this kind of thing.

      I see what you're getting at! How meaningful is it to measure the "pain" of a broken arm in terms of hit points loss, though? Is there any good way to quantify that?
      \nYes, simply think: "which would you rather?". This analysis was more than a little inspired by Berlekamp's environmental go
      The weight differences are typically so extreme that it makes the choices easier ("is it more losing 5 HP, or 22?"). The system typically doesn't ask you to quantify it precisely, just in very big bands.\n
      You're right about many (most?) hits going for a "maximum" effective spend, in theory. With this hack, I would limit hit point expenditure to 4 per roll. Characters should have fairly few hit points to start with, as well.
      \nOk, that makes my code a lot faster and the table a lot shorter and more decisive.
      Basically always spend 4, if getting a 6 or lower will be as painful as losing as little as three or more HP, otherwise spend 0. Never ever spend 1 or 2.\n
      (((0 0 0) (0 0)) ((3 0 0) (4 1.22)))
      \n
      I don't know if it's enough, having said all that.
      \nThose things only affect the weights, making HP spends that more painful, leading to more 0:s and fewer 4:s, not to any 1:s or 2:s. The same principle stands, even in the dragon scratch/breath example and even with 2 of the 4 HP being lost permanently.
      Is taking your chances with rolling + 0 strictly less painful than losing three or more HP (1.5 of those permanently)? Then spend 0. Otherwise spend 4.

      There's no need for finer quantification. The player's guesstimation that the fictional consequences are bad as 3 or more, or not as bad as 3 or more? That's it.\n
      As for "action movie injuries" - do you visualize all D&D fights as a string of misses, then?
      \nAs a series of skillful parries, yes. They're not missing, it's just being parried or dodged, think fencing.
      My mental model misses out on the "took a wound but kept fighting" possibilities, any wound beyond the most surface of nicks, and you go down. That's a loss, I know, but it's the closest I could map a mental model to the RAW.

      Ye olden model is more like a woodcutting model. Chop, chop, chop until the tree falls. That's not great either.

      Obv games exist with a built in model that makes sense -- I was knocking Fate upthread for its retcon tendencies but its wound/consequence system makes a lot of sense. But I'm thinking in terms of "how can I make HP/AC as implemented in 5e make sense in my head", not "How can I have the best wound model" (in which case many other games would do it better, including Fate). Even my model has some iffy spots, like the various resistances or immunities to damage types (solved as "Less bothered by fire" or whatever) and the name of the Cure Wounds spell. Which is there for nostalgia.

      If so, wouldn't it be more logical to increase AC with levels instead of HP?
      \nAC does increase with levels but so does HP. It's risky to increase AC because you get into some sorta superman territory where no amount of villagers can pose a threat to you. HP goes up as you get more trained and get more smiled upon by fate.\n
      And assign damage ratings based on how easy something is to avoid? For instance, a swarm of insects should do more "damage" than a 2-ton boulder thrown from a great height.
      \nThe 2-ton boulder is more likely to be fight-ending.
      And the swarm typically does do more damage unless the boulder is fight-ending.
      The damage ratings are assigned based on how badly you don't want to get hit by it.
      You don't want to die a boulder death? Then you better have a lot of hitpoints.

      Actually, it now strikes me how similar vanilla RAW in my mental model is to your system!
      In your system, the opponent tries to harm you, which you can scoff at if you're dextrous or armed enough. If you're not, you can roll on the 'oh shit' tables (first roll to resist, then roll to recover). Spending HP (always 4) gives you better odds on these.

      In "my" system, the opponent tries to harm you, which you can scoff at if you're dextrous or armed enough. If you're not, you can roll on the 'oh shit' tables (first roll death saves to see if you even live, then roll to see if you sustain a real injury). Spending HP (which is mandatory, and the more dangerous attacks have a higher HP cost) makes it so you even don't have to go to those tables. You got lucky and can keep standing.

      I think the biggest difference is that you also go unconscious at the "oh shit" stage of "my" system, when you're out of HP. Also, you can insta-die! Take your current+max HP in damage in one hit and you're a goner!\n
      It seems to me that fantasy novels and such have just as strong a tradition of "scary-sounding injury that the character can pretty much ignore until later" as action movies.
      \nThat's not something I'm super familiar with, I'm thinking Zorro, Robin Hood, Lord of the Rings. You fight until touch\xe9, someone's down -- possibly gruesomely.\n
      I do agree that it wouldn't work all that well in a D&D-esque game where lots of "blows" are landed in the course of a fight. Much better something AW-or-WoD-esque, where an injury is a fairly rare and significant thing*. (And you can afford to maybe get hit once before you go down for good.)
      \nOur injuries have a chance of being plenty serious. The broken rib eventually led to that character dying. The lost foot, too, because the character could no longer have both a shield and a weapon because of the cane.

      \n Again, Paul, always a pleasure to talk to you and David about things like this, feels like we can hear each other out and bounce ideas back and forth.

      Rephrasing the AC/HP thing:
      AC (and saves) is about how likely it is to be avoided completely.
      HP is about how dangerous it is if you can't avoid it.
      As the characters level up, they can meet bigger and bigger dangers without having the probabilities change that much. They can fight more and more goblins at once. If AC were to increase instead, the goblins would no longer be a threat at all or tiring at all, except for nat 20's. Which would be lethal.
      The narrow range, we discussed in the target number thread also.

      \n Well, we're getting a bit off-topic now...

      My issue with D&D's AC/HP system has always been simply that it's very difficult to connect to any kind of meaningful way of understanding what's happening in the fiction.

      Is AC about being nimble and avoiding attacks? Yes! It can go up if you're very agile or very small.

      But, no, AC can also go up if you're large and easy to hit, but heavily armoured. Like a big, slow tortoise (or even a "soft" target with few vital spots!).

      So, what does that mean? It means that the AC number tells us *nothing*, really, except how hard it is to reduce your hit points in combat. The "to-hit" roll doesn't tell us anything about whether you got hit (because you're heavily armoured) or not (because you were agile). Dang! Why do we bother rolling to-hit, then? ...

      Well, ok. Let's ignore that for now. So, then, let's skip to the next step: what does having your hit points reduced in combat mean?

      Well, it turns out we don't know what that means, either. You can get more hit points by being really big and tough, or from healing. However, you can lose them just by being in the vicinity of dangerous stuff or getting lucky and dodging a sniper's arrow (since we've already determined that losing hit points does NOT mean you got hit). If hit points were just about your ability to dodge things, then why does it take a long time and bandages for you to get them back? How can you draw advantage from your hit points if you're immobile? Hmmm.

      In the end, we're left with a system with all these numbers and all these rolls... but it turns out that none of them actually tell us ANYTHING concrete about what's happening in the fiction itself. All they do is tell us when numbers on the character sheet change (numbers which we have already decided are completely abstract).

      Well, gee. In practice this can be just fine (hey, we can ignore the fact that we're dealing with abstractions!) in some situations and completely useless or even plain wrong in others. (Should a poison be handled as extra HP loss or a save vs. Death? It's basically impossible to say. However, it could be an absolutely CRUCIAL point, making the difference between life and death for one character vs. another. That puts the GM in a very awkward position if they have to make a call about this kind of thing.)

      Given an approach to playing D&D that's trying to remain somewhat objective and "let the dice roll what they roll", in the open, like you like to do (Sandra), it presents a lot of difficulties. "Did that poisoned arrow hit the princess, or not?" The system isn't helping us with that *one bit*. It's all in your hands to say whether it did or not (except in those moments where hit points reach 0, of course).

      Sure, you can, as you say, occasionally choose to narrate HP loss as a non-lethal blow. But you're doing so purely arbitrarily, based on a whim and what seems appropriate. There's no "objective" or internally consistent way to do so, based on (lack of) logic of the AC/HP system. There's no "roll the dice in the open and trust in the gloracle" method here, no matter how much you squint.

      With something like my hack here, you get that objectivity back, as well as clear fictional cues. Yes, that arrow hit you. Now, will you roll to resist its effects? Ok, looks like the poison on its isn't not slowing you down now, but it WILL affect you after the fight. (You'll have to roll to recover then, to see how bad it is.) Great! We can all immediately imagine what that looks like and make appropriate decisions about what's happening in the fiction and then draw logical conclusions from that. ("As soon as this fight is over, let's get her to the healer - maybe he has an antidote!") That's important to me.
      \n

      \n Back on-topic:

      I wonder how strong/convincing this math is. Sometimes mathematical breakpoints exist, but human beings still prefer to make different decisions for psychological reasons. A great deal of playtesting would be necessary to see!

      Now, I believe the argument you are making here is that people should generally (to follow optimal behaviour) either spend 0 or 4 points. That means a person getting hit will be rolling +4, +4, +4, +x, +0 (where "x" is a remainder which is less than 4).

      If that is truly an optimal set of behaviour AND one which is appealing to people psychologically, could it be fixed in some very simple fashion?

      For instance, instead of:\n

      Hit Points
      --------
      [] [] [] [] [] [] [] [] [] [] []
      \nWe have something like this:\n

      Hit Points
      --------
      +3 [] []
      +2 [] [] []
      +1 [] [] [] []
      \nYou check off a box to roll with that bonus. Now we should get a full range of rolls, instead of just +4s and +0s. Yes/no? This is off the top of my head.

      Well, we're getting a bit off-topic now...
      \nSome of the close to 600 posts I made were on topic
      I spend hours and hours doing the math and writing it up before we strayed to this side topic and I wanted to keep the side topic about similarities and differences to your proposed system, which I think it has been. That's not meant to be as defensive as it sounds, I enjoy talking about this.
      Unlike a certain herculean topic on another site.\n
      Is AC about being nimble and avoiding attacks? Yes! It can go up if you're very agile or very small.

      But, no, AC can also go up if you're large and easy to hit, but heavily armoured. Like a big, slow tortoise (or even a "soft" target with few vital spots!).
      \nRight, that's why I wrote dextrous or armored. I know this has some problems but it brings everything into one roll unlike GURPs' three rolls (attack, dodge/parry, DR). It's a trade-off. The names could be better too instead of these boat hull names, "armor class" "hit points".\n
      So, what does that mean? It means that the AC number tells us *nothing*, really, except how hard it is to reduce your hit points in combat. The "to-hit" roll doesn't tell us anything about whether you got hit (because you're heavily armoured) or not (because you were agile). Dang! Why do we bother rolling to-hit, then? ...
      \nThis is a similarity between "my" system and yours, though. If you can out-AC their hit, you can scoff at their attack. If you can't, then it's "Oh, shit, this is serious!" time. And HP is the only thing that stands in between the PC and the "oh shit, serious" tables in both our systems.

      In your system, you spend your choice of either 4 or 0 and if you do spend 4, you get better odds on the "os,s"-tables.
      In "my" system, you (have to) spend an amount of HP determined by the danger the attack presents. You're not nimble enough to withstand a giant boulder? You better have a lot of luck saved up ("luck" = HP) or you're going to the "os,s"-tables!

      And in both our systems you can come out pretty ok on the "os,s" tables if you're lucky. The mildest consequence on the DMG's lingering injuries table is "minor scar, no adverse effect, can be removed by magical healing of level 6 or higher" and the mildest consequence on the death tables is unconscious one hour.\n
      Well, ok. Let's ignore that for now. So, then, let's skip to the next step: what does having your hit points reduced in combat mean?
      \nYour luck is running out!\n
      Well, it turns out we don't know what that means, either.
      \nYou have a point that AC represents a mix of pinging and whiffing (and just plain bad ass parrying and fencing). And you're right that HP has the same issue! Fatigue, luck, will-to-live, surface scratches...\n
      You can get more hit points by being really big and tough, or from healing. However, you can lose them just by being in the vicinity of dangerous stuff or getting lucky and dodging a sniper's arrow (since we've already determined that losing hit points does NOT mean you got hit).
      \nThat's right, those things are tiring, exhausting, scary, makes you lose your footing, your breath, lowers your odds of winning in the fight, all of those things and also the final thing which is a little copouty: you're using up your luck, it's like "Oh, that cat just lost one of her nine lives" thing.\n
      If hit points were just about your ability to dodge things, then why does it take a long time and bandages for you to get them back? How can you draw advantage from your hit points if you're immobile? Hmmm.
      \nI got to this model of hitpoints precisely because it doesn't take a long time to get back anymore, you can get some HP back in an hour's rest and all of it in one night's rest.\n
      Given an approach to playing D&D that's trying to remain somewhat objective and "let the dice roll what they roll", in the open, like you like to do (Sandra), it presents a lot of difficulties. "Did that poisoned arrow hit the princess, or not?" The system isn't helping us with that *one bit*. It's all in your hands to say whether it did or not (except in those moments where hit points reach 0, of course).
      \nI agree, this is important.
      That's why some of the attacks have hard-coded outcomes on overcoming the AC of the opponent (confusingly called "on hit") and some have hard-coded outcomes on reducing the HP to zero.
      Like a vampire; if they "hit" (overcome the AC) with their claws [and specifically with their claws], they can choose between reducing HP (the claws are scary) or imposing a grapple condition.
      If they reduce the opponent to 0 with their bite [and specifically with their bite], the opponent dies and becomes a vampire also.

      One to-hit-rider that's used sometimes in the Monster Manual is to reduce the HP maximum in addition to just reducing HP. (Often used for undead draining energy from their foes, the vampire bite has this property in addition to the reduce-to-0-rider.)

      So the system does a good job of saying "this HP loss has this flavor, that HP loss has that flavor, this AC-overcoming has this flavor, that AC-overcoming has that flavor" and making them mechanically distinct.

      Poison attacks in the Monster Manual are typically just extra damage (in which case the princess dodged the arrow, but at a higher HP cost since it was a more significant attack) or they have a rider on some specified part of the attack process.

      But I'm very glad you brought up the princess & the poisoned arrow example because I'm running an OSR module and there's, lets just say there's gonna be some poisoned arrows in there, and since the text wasn't written for 5e specifically it has this vagueness problem. I'm going to have to decide way ahead of next session whether the effects that this OSR author wrote up are on-hit-riders or on-reduce-to-0-riders or some other compromise triggers.

      So I just looked in the DMG for the poisons in its list and most of them have the same mechanic: to determine whether or not the princess was poisoned or not, there is an additional saving throw made. Phew, I'm going to use that for the poison arrows in this module: if the target's AC is overcome, make save, if they made the save they dodged it. I like that mechanic even though can read as a little retconny. I'll have to make sure to place the save in an appropriate moment in the IIEE. Thanks for making me think about this, Paul!\n
      Sure, you can, as you say, occasionally choose to narrate HP loss as a non-lethal blow. But you're doing so purely arbitrarily, based on a whim and what seems appropriate.
      \nRight but we typically only do it when it's clear that we can afford such whims. Like on a big owlbear that's almost fallen over, or a PC who wants to voluntarily add a "clench your teeth" moment. Like, this is something that we've done like three or four times over the last two years. But you're right, it's confusing to blend in some of these surface wounds in with the "fatigue/luck" -- I was offering it as a concession "yeah it's confusing" rather than as a "look how good it is that it can be done this way too".

      \nOh, sorry, Paul! I would've been way briefer in my last post if I had known you were still on this topic, which is the part of the topic that's the most interesting anyway.\n
      I wonder how strong/convincing this math is. Sometimes mathematical breakpoints exist, but human beings still prefer to make different decisions for psychological reasons. A great deal of playtesting would be necessary to see!
      \nThat's right, playtesting is important but this principle was discovered in playtesting by Aviatrix and her crew in a similar game (Gumshoe).

      Winning Ways for your Mathematical Plays is also important

      Like, in 5e, one breakpoint that is apparent to the naked eye is that odd ability scores kinda are bad. A 15 is so much more expensive than a 14 but in most cases do the exact same thing. (The biggest benefit, and this is certainly not nothing, is that a 15 is easier than 14 to eventually train it up to a 16, which is significantly better. Otherwise, it's only strength that have some fringe and rare benefits of a 15 over a 14. In the houserules I'm working on for our next game, charisma is going to get a new set of breakpoints for some uses (3, 4, 6, 9, 13, 16, 18) alongside the old "all even numbers" breakpoints for the existing 5e uses. Oh I'm going off topic again. I'm sorry. But this is just to convey that these breakpoints are kinda inevitable in a g\xf6delian way.\n
      Now, I believe the argument you are making here is that people should generally (to follow optimal behaviour) either spend 0 or 4 points. That means a person getting hit will be rolling +4, +4, +4, +x, +0 (where "x" is a remainder which is less than 4).
      \nCorrect. With the occasional +0 thrown in there -- maybe most of the time (outnumbering the fours by a large margin), maybe very rarely or never.\n
      If that is truly an optimal set of behaviour AND one which is appealing to people psychologically, could it be fixed in some very simple fashion?
      \nOne way to make the "always +4" thing less unsatifsying can be to lampshade it. To just note in the text that "Often it's best to either spend the full +4, or to not spend anything". I think for me one of the most unsatisfying things is when you notice a breakpoint like this and it seems like the designer didn't think of it.\n
      For instance, instead of:\n

      Hit Points
      --------
      [] [] [] [] [] [] [] [] [] [] []
      \nWe have something like this:\n

      Hit Points
      --------
      +3 [] []
      +2 [] [] []
      +1 [] [] [] []
      \nYou check off a box to roll with that bonus. Now we should get a full range of rolls, instead of just +4s and +0s. Yes/no? This is off the top of my head.
      \nInteresting!
      You should get the threes first and then the twos and then the ones. But that can still be an interesting thing happening in the game. The character is getting more and more run down and less and less able to control her harmful fate.

      Because if something is mild enough that you can even afford to save a three, you're so likely to overspend or underspend if you spend +1 or +2, because the weight is so low, it's better to spend 0.

      Actually in a system with greater granularity, like Numenera, (because bigger numbers), there are lots of breakpoints where with one weight, it's either "spend 3 or spend 0, if you have 1 or 2, save them" and at a slightly higher weight, there's "spend 3, if you can't afford that, spend 2 or 1". Hence the much bigger tables I posted upthread, they have these orders for weights when things switch order between "spend either max or zero" or "spend it all". That doesn't solve or even alleviate the problem though. It just means that you have to have a big ole number table in front of you rather than a simple "4 or 0" rule.

      \n Yes, very interesting! I really appreciate the thought you've given to the mathematics (although I'm not *entirely* sure just how powerful the breakpoint effect is here without having some more data - for instance, how it interacts with a "recovery" roll).

      I think that D&D hit points only make sense if you are willing to consider them an abstract entity: some combination of luck, karma, and "plot immunity". And yes, this DOES cause a bunch of interesting (and, to me, problematic) issues in play if you're trying to remain objective and make reliable and consistent calls. For instance, consider that if a poisoned arrow means you lose more hit points when trying to dodge it, then you get a weird "quantum effect" where, hypothetically, a character who didn't know it was poisoned would be "expending more effort" to avoid the arrow for no apparent reason.

      If you assume some kind of bizarre and magical reason (like cosmic karma or fate), then I suppose it's fine; but when you're trying to make important decisions in play (like your own example with the princess, using the saving throw), it doesn't help you one bit. In that case, for instance, your house rule would impact one character (who has tons of hit points but bad saves) very differently from another (low hit points, high saves), and at some level, once you're aware of that, you can't "unsee it". The call you make will advantage one character over another for no real "in-game" reason - it's just an artifact of the resolution rules. I find that problematic if I'm GMing, and would rather distance myself from it.

      I can see a few solutions to this conundrum (I'd love to hear if there are others):

      1. Assume that none of these numbers represent anything "real" at all (at best, the currents of fate or spirits interfering behind the scenes to keep your character alive), and embrace the abstract nature. This, like I said, can be problematic for making rules calls without developing bias.

      That's the approach I took with this hack:

      Given this hack, there is absolutely NO ambiguity: it is crystal clear to everyone that hit points are an abstract resource and do not represent anything. I really like that clarity, myself - I feel like it would save me a lot of trouble and time at the table.

      2. Your approach: no one is really "hit" by anything until their hit points run out, and that's when an injury happens, when a blow lands. This is a pretty sensible approach! I like it.

      It also brings problems, in my opinion, though minor ones:

      * How come characters know when their "luck" is running out? (e.g. you're at 1 hit point) Is there any meaningful way to present this in-character? ("Uh, guys, I'm feeling tired." "Ok, here, have some coffee!" "Uh, no. That won't help. You just don't understand, do you, mother?!?")

      * It's not possible to create a situation where a character survives something by pure physical resilience. What if I want my character's special feature to be that he's incredibly tough, like Wolverine? It's not obvious how to negotiate this in the fiction (without also making him more susceptible to "on hit" effects).

      This could come up if, say, a character with a lot of hit points becomes elected to the presidency. They have 100 hit points, and they're giving an address/speech, standing immobile on a podium in front of a crowd. A sniper dealing 1d6 damage per shot is trying to take them down. What does this even look like in the fiction? If the sniper's aim is bad or conditions are bad, we know: they keep missing, over and over. But what if that's not in question? For instance, the sniper is an amazing marksman who never misses?

      These sound like oddball scenarios, sometimes, but I seem them fairly regularly. For instance, it would be nice to have a magical arrow that never misses its target. If I fire that arrow at the president, what happens?

      These situations can get frustrating for a group, I find. Does the president swat it away with his hand at the last minute? Does some weird coincidence take place, like a pigeon happening to fly between the arrow and the target?

      3. In my D&D "hack" I'm trying out the opposite approach: hit points ARE pretty much just physical resilience. If you're losing hit points, you've been hit.

      That means that hit points rarely increase, and only within small bounds. Someone with a lot of hit points has an "action movie hero" kind of vibe, where they can just shrug off a couple of injuries and keep on going. But that's fairly rare; most people go down when hit once or twice. (That's the sweet spot for me in a game like D&D anyway.)

      I became attracted to this approach when I understood that old-school D&D had 1d6 hit points for most entities, and 1d6 damage for most weapons: in other words, we're not counting resilience and damage! It's just a randomizer to see whether a particular blow kills someone or not (and the odds are about 50%!). Now that makes a lot more sense!

      For monsters and such, which should have a lot of hit points, I'm assigning hp based on the size of the beastie. If it's a small creature which should nevertheless be very "tough", then they get a pool of hit points with a "description".

      For instance:

      * Incredibly alert and agile - 20 hit points.
      * Magical armour spell, active whenever expecting danger - 12 hit points.

      Normally these just add to total hit points, but if the PCs find a way to bypass them (e.g. holding them still, or an anti-magic effect), then I ignore them. Seems more interesting from an adventure point of view, and avoids some of these same problems. Arguably a bit more complex, but that doesn't bother me - it's still fairly simple, at least in my mind.

      \nIf you're interested in reading a whole lot more of a "hit points" discussion, and potentially continuing it further, I'd recommend looking at a discussion we had about this which got quite in-depth. It's here, on the last couple of pages in particular:

      Writing up Eero's Primordial...

      If we're going to continue, it might make more sense to move the discussion over there!

      Here's an example of something that came out of it on my end:

      \n
      Why even bother with HP at all?

      -Everything does 1d6 damage.
      -Level 1 Magic Users die (or Save Vs Death/Roll on Wounds table) on damage of 4 or greater, thieves/clerics on 5+ and fighters on 6+.
      -Wearing armour gives you an Armour Save before death rolls (geez, this is getting       Warhammer pretty fast).
      -Higher levels increases the Damage Save score above 6 at some point. Powerful attacks deal 2d6 damage.

      There you go. I think I could probably run a dungeon crawl with those rules. Beermat Character Sheets for playing at pubs.
      \nI've always been fond of this kind of rule for D&D-esque game design. However, you have a bit of problem when a series of lucky rolls can have your 1st-level Fighter surviving 12 blows from a frightening monster, with no reduction in future chances of death. I mean, it's not necessarily a *problem*, but it might feel a bit funny that this same dude can go take *another* arrow in the chest with only a 1-in-6 chance of snuffing it.

      I would do it like this, personally (and in D&D-esque games, I tend to adopt a rule like this for monsters instead of tracking hit points in any case):

      * Everything does 1d6 damage. (Or maybe really scary things are d6+1, d6+2... EDIT: roll extra dice and keep the best! That's a better match here.)

      When you get hit, check the amount of damage against your personal scores:

      * You die if the damage total is...
      - Magic-User: 3+level (i.e. 4+ for 1st-level)
      - Thief/Cleric: 4+level (i.e. 5+ for 1st-level)
      - Fighter: 5+level (i.e. 6+ for 1st-level)

      * You are wounded if the damage total is...
      - Unarmoured: 1+
      - Wearing armour: 2+
      - Wearing really heavy armour: 3+


      When you are wounded, decrease your "if you die" number by 1 until you have a chance to heal your wounds.

      This models current levels of deadliness in standard D&D pretty well, actually. A third-level Thief with 3d6 hit points can take two to four hits before going down. Under this system, he has a "dead" number of 7+, so the first hit is just an injury, the second kills him on a 6, and the third on a 5-6, and the fourth on a 4-6. (Cumulatively, 17% chance of dying on the second blow, 44% chance of dying with the third blow, and 72% chance of dying with the fourth blow. With D&D hit points, assuming the Thief has 10.5 hit points, that would be a 0% chance of death on the first blow, a 17% chance on the second, 62% on the third.)

      If you want to work Consitution and stuff like that into this, have it come into play in the "healing" part of the rules. Maybe you normally only heal overnight under safe conditions, but a Constitution check can "heal" one level of damage even in a short rest or under inhospitable circumstances. Then you can still have "hardy" characters, who seem to bounce back easily from an injury, versus "sickly" ones, who need extended bed-rest to do the same.

      \nI think the thing is that I don't really see how your hack solves any of the points you bring up? They look to me to be very similar in degree of association/disassociation.
      The main difference is that in vanilla RAW D&D you get unconscious when you go to zero.

      Otherwise it's the same thing, isn't it? It's so close to being the exact same thing mechanically.
      * How come characters know when their "luck" is running out? (e.g. you're at 1 hit point) Is there any meaningful way to present this in-character? ("Uh, guys, I'm feeling tired." "Ok, here, have some coffee!" "Uh, no. That won't help. You just don't understand, do you, mother?!?")
      \nBut it does help!

      5e has a mechanic that's like this: sit down, have some coffee, and then you can move some HP over from a reserve pool to your main pool (not the words they use, but...). The main pool replenishesh fully with a full night's sleep, and the reserve pool is half replenished (so in two nights, well three maybe because it's "half, rounded down", you've replenished that too).
      Also some drinks, even non magical drinks, regain HP. Like there's a strong cider (or something) you can find in one dungeon and if you drink just a little, you regain 1 HP. If you drink alot, you get drunk and get disadvantage on all rolls.

      * It's not possible to create a situation where a character survives something by pure physical resilience. What if I want my character's special feature to be that he's incredibly tough, like Wolverine? It's not obvious how to negotiate this in the fiction (without also making him more susceptible to "on hit" effects).

      This could come up if, say, a character with a lot of hit points becomes elected to the presidency.       These sound like oddball scenarios, sometimes, but I seem them fairly regularly. For instance, it would be nice to have a magical arrow that never misses its target. If I fire that arrow at the president, what happens?
      \nYeah, it completely breaks down then. An ordinary rifle deals just 2d10 in 5e. So it becomes another way to miss and it's always a sign of klugy design when TIMTOWTDI. I agree.
      But the game can create similar stories, like the spell Power Word Kill: "You utter a word of power that can compel one creature you can see to die instantly."
      Weapons and arrows can be created to mimic this spell? Even though the default magic arrow just is a +1 to hit and damage. That doesn't have to be the be-all, end-all.\n
      These situations can get frustrating for a group, I find. Does the president swat it away with his hand at the last minute? Does some weird coincidence take place, like a pigeon happening to fly between the arrow and the target?
      \nWe probably just laugh about it and move on? The HP mechanic is weird.

      Also, some of these can be examples where you might want to make an exception. Like Wolverine, she can (or he can) describe the attacks as really being hit, but being so tough. The system has room for it. Only "lingering injuries" from the table are the real injuries.
      Again, your "it wasn't so bad" thing? The system has room for that, too. I offered this as a concession of confusion earlier but it sounds like to the sort of gritty actiony stories you want to tell, it's a big pro. Use the variant in the DMG where every crit, instead of every time you go to zero, causes a lingering injury?
      For me, I don't really miss the "took a wound but kept fighting" story moments.\n
      3. In my D&D "hack" I'm trying out the opposite approach: hit points ARE pretty much just physical resilience. If you're losing hit points, you've been hit.

      That means that hit points rarely increase, and only within small bounds. Someone with a lot of hit points has an "action movie hero" kind of vibe, where they can just shrug off a couple of injuries and keep on going. But that's fairly rare; most people go down when hit once or twice. (That's the sweet spot for me in a game like D&D anyway.)

      I became attracted to this approach when I understood that old-school D&D had 1d6 hit points for most entities, and 1d6 damage for most weapons: in other words, we're not counting resilience and damage! It's just a randomizer to see whether a particular blow kills someone or not (and the odds are about 50%!). Now that makes a lot more sense!
      \nThat's how it was at low levels. You had 1d6 per level.
      In ye olden days, fighters died after one hit and you rolled to hit. Then they invented heroes that died after four hits and super heroes that died after eight hits. This was before D&D. (Those later became fighter level 4 and fighter level 8 respectively, and they changed "one hit" to "one d6".)
      You die after four hits, maybe the president-elect is a hero?

      I think that if I would've invented a game I would've made you get a wound (or die) everytime you got hit. Not bother with this luck-warping "Hit Points" And it would've been a lot less fun to play than D&D.
      I don't want to hack too much, I think that's the crux. I have no qualms ripping out an entire subsystem and replacing it with something from another game or something I've designed, and maybe one day I'll do that for wounds and hitting, but I think what we have has so far provided great experience at play.\n
      Normally these just add to total hit points, but if the PCs find a way to bypass them (e.g. holding them still, or an anti-magic effect)
      \n3e D&D had a lot of this with a long list of ACs (one if you're unaware of the attack (AC minus the dodge part), one if the attack ignores your armor (AC with only the dodge part) and a whole bunch of others, I think four in total including normal AC). I'm also enjoying 5e because it's simple and fast!
      You're fighting along, whittling each other's abstract HP pool down and then bam! Oh no! You might die! Time to roll death saves and lingering injury!
      But the system is just a mechanical baseline with lots of interesting (and sometimes weird) exceptions, like the aforementioned vampire bite.

      It does the "fence parry fence parry riposte parry fence oh no I died" thing well, with "missed rolls" representing a well parried attack and "hit rolls" representing attacks that cause you a bit more doubt.

      And it does the insect swarm well -- the bugs are very likely to affect your ability to continue fighting without completely stopping you.
      And it does the 2-ton-rock well -- it either ends the fight quickly, or it's sidestepped causually, or it's a major shock.

      It does make significantly less sense in situations where God made a rock too heavy to lift or the danger of the attack is unknown (like secret poison) and some of these situations are common, some are uncommon. But D&D is like Achilles' record player. Most songs sound great on it.

      You've made a system where you can afford to get hit just a couple of times over your life unless you get significant magical healing or resurrection.
      And a system with that property -- that's what I already have and use and love.

      I think in OD&D, HP absolutely represented... well.. it says:

      "[HP] being the number of points of damage the character could sustain before death. Whether sustaining accumulative hits will otherwise affect a character is left to the discretion of the referee."
      and
      "All attacks which score hits do 1-6 damage unless otherwise noted."

      So that's the nostalgia history of OD&D.
      To-hit-rolls that lead to a damage roll that lead to dimishing HP that lead to death.
      That's the mechanical core.
      What we then wrap around that in terms of imagined space, and what mechanics we build around that, well, that can be done a thousand ways or you can use some completely other system of wounds and consequences and injuries.

      I've gone for a kinda veiled take on the violence. It becomes real when it becomes real, sure, but until HP 0, it's just fun and games. Shields and mystical auras and fancy footwork and parrying blades and "phew, that was close!"

      We see a lot of character death in our games as it is.
      Those death save rolls and that instant-death rule see a lot of use.

      That's right, playtesting is important but this principle was discovered in playtesting by Aviatrix and her crew in a similar game (Gumshoe).
      \nI'm only a bystander in this conversation, but the optimal spend issue in Gumshoe was driven home to me more by an article that specifically referenced Night's Black Agents; this was the first place I really thought about the truth it expressed.

      Once you do that, it's obvious that the utility value of a points spent on a failed roll is at best zero, so now as part of my Gumshoe orientation I strongly encourage players to spend either 3 points or 0.

      The Stability system was where this really showed the fraying, because here you actually did have a weighting, in that there is a potential Stability loss for a failed roll. This means that for any given test there will be an optimax solution, and since Stability is crucial to being able to do things, most people spent some time trying to find the solution. In practice, of course, it basically worked out to "Spend 0, 3, or 4 points." I decided to shortcut all that and codify the point tax with a "fixed cost" chart that already worked out the optimax spend. In play, this speeds things up considerably.

      It is true that my conversation about this inspired Sandra's math. I fully expect a citation on her Fields Prize

      \n Basically in 5e: Your HP is 1d8+con per level. (Well, the die type is different for each class but let's go with rogue. And also the first level you max the die instead of roll it.)

      And you also have a pool of hit dice (all D&D veterans collectively rolled their eyes at the re-appropriated name, deservedly so). In the case of rogue, one d8 per level.

      Now, here is how it works:
      Short rest: spend any number of your hit dice and add in your con to them all, and add that to your current HP. You can roll them one at a time if you so wish.

      Long rest: your current HP is reset to your max HP and you regain some of your hit dice. (At level 1, you regain one hit die per night and have max 1. At level 7, you regain three hit dice per night and have max 7. And so on -- it's half, rounded down.)

      So in our game the characters are sitting down, having some tea, scratching off rations, and rolling their hit dice.

      The default is short rest = one hour. Long rest = one night. I love this.

      Variants proposed in the text are the more 4e like short rest = 5 minutes, long rest = 1 hour,
      and the more B/X-like short rest = one night, long rest = one week.
      I can see using either but I'm very happy with the default.
      Again, when we were playing B/X, we were getting free CLW from the clerics in the city every day anyway (if we even got hurt, we mostly avoided combat because it was so lethal) so the long rest = one night fits well to what I'm used to from it.
      \n
      \n

      I read the link to the analysis of GUMSHOE - really more of an advice column than mathematical analysis - and I'm wondering how relevant it is to this thing we're discussing here.
      \nTo be clear:

      I did the mathematical analysis after talking to Aviatrix; she and her crew started playing with 3 and 0 spends after that article.
      The article didn't replace the analysis, and I hadn't even heard of the article.
      It went: article -> playtesting -> analysis.

      I doubted the playtesting but the analysis confirmed the playtesting.

      \n Well, I do understand the basic idea:

      Essentially, the principle is that if you really DON'T want to miss a roll, you're better off spending as much as you can. Because if you spend less, you will most likely miss the roll AND lose the points. If you decide it's not worth it, you shouldn't spend any points.

      Another house rule which could address this would be an automatic 1pt spend any time you have to make the roll.

      In any case, this is very thought-provoking. I wonder how the math would change if the adds weren't linear? In other words, if spending the points were a diminishing returns kind of thing. If the curve was steep enough, it might affect the "recommended behaviour"...

      \n

    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\n2097 \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJune 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      Well, I do understand the basic idea:

      Essentially, the principle is that if you really DON'T want to miss a roll, you're better off spending as much as you can. Because if you spend less, you will most likely miss the roll AND lose the points. If you decide it's not worth it, you shouldn't spend any points.

      Another house rule which could address this would be an automatic 1pt spend any time you have to make the roll.

      In any case, this is very thought-provoking. I wonder how the math would change if the adds weren't linear? In other words, if spending the points were a diminishing returns kind of thing. If the curve was steep enough, it might affect the "recommended behaviour"...
      \nNumenera does this, well, they use increasing returns instead of diminishing returns. Their name for the number you subtract from the difficulty (actually the same as adding it to the die) is "effort" and then thee first "effort" per roll costs 3 from your "pool", all the following costs 2.

      What happens? That the formula stops caring about pool and starts caring about effort, leaving the exchange rates to the player.
      I.e it becomes "If failing the roll is as painful as paying for X effort, you should pay for Y effort, otherwise Z effort", where Y quickly becomes "as much as you can" and Z quickly zero.[*]

      In short: no change.

      The 2d6 bell curve already creates a diminishing returns effect, and this effect makes the table more collapsed, not less. Combined with the 4-spend max it quickly becomes 4 or 0. Whereas a linear curve might have some weights left where 1, 2 or 3 would be best. That wouldn't make it any better though, you'd just need a look-up table in front of you.

      As for the automatic one hp loss each roll, that shouldn't change anything either. You can't win that HP back. And you can't choose whether or not to roll.
      It becomes exactly the same math and tables as before, only that you now have one HP less to spend from -- the math isn't dependant on your current amount of hit points (except to determine when you can't spend it all.)


      [*]Numenera does become a little more nuanced but that's because of other factors, not this separation between "effort" and pool spend. Numenera requires a ternary lookup table: the difficulty (not always 7-9, it can be set by the DM or by the game's circumstances to be 1-10), the weight, and then finally how much you even can spend since you have a ridiculously small pool size making it impossible to reduce the difficulty to one. So depending on the weight of the roll you should spend it all or spend zero -- but where that weight is changes depending on what "it all" is. If you only have 1 left, the weight it requires for you to spend it is higher than if you only have 4 left and so on.

      But, I have created this table. It only makes the game more broken in my opinion.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\n2097 \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJune 2016 \n edited June 2016
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n So there's not really a choice when it comes to how much HP to spend, it's just that the breakpoints in some games can become very many and it becomes a little complicated.
      It happened to become easy in PbtA-hacked because of the bell curve, the fixed difficulty and the 4-cap. It happened to become easy in Gumshoe because of the coarse grained die and Aviatrix' house rule setting the difficulty to a fixed 4.
      But having it complicated is no virtue; it doesn't make it any less "scripted".

      So instead of having a fa\xe7ade of voluntary point spend, I suggest two little changes to your hack:
      1. When you get hurt, you mandantorily lose an amount of points that varies to the degree of danger involved in the attack. This will create a nuanced and unpredictable point loss instead of the static ticks of 0, 4, 0, 0, 4, 4, 4 (or in case of always losing 1: 1, 4, 1, 1, 4, 4, 4). Your HP becomes something you care about and you don't really know how fast it'll be gone. Something you want to manage but it can fly away like birds in the night at a moment's notice.

      2. And then, instead of bothering with the extra step of rolling dice, you can just clench teeth and be tough -- as long as you had HP to lose. If you're at zero, only then do you have to make the dangerous rolls, risking a serious consequence or death. This creates the action moments that you desire.
      \n

      \n Interesting! That makes me wonder if you're completely misunderstanding how my hack works.

      For instance, you do understand that hit points are not related to things like going unconscious and/or being injured *at all*?

      You could have all your hit points and die.

      You could have lost all your hit points and be perfectly functional.

      Yes/no?

      EDIT: Thinking about it a bit further, I can see that if (!) you see hit points as purely abstract, meaning that no one necessarily gets "hit" at all until they're gone, and THEN you use some kind of roll to determine whether you are injured or not, AND this injury or lack of injury does not "heal" with the restoration of hit points, then there ARE a lot of similarities. That just hasn't been my experience of how people play D&D... well, not even once, and I've played quite a bit of D&D over the years.

      \n
      \n Yes -- and I have a couple of times called out that difference explicitly

      Yes to that they're similar other than that and a couple of other differences.
      Your "if/then/and/are" describes my thinking well, seems like we're on the same page. HP protects you, when it's depleted, you roll to see if you die or live. If live, you roll to see what injury (and the mildest being "minor scar") and for each injury it says what spells are needed to restore it or disguise it -- just restoring HP doesn't help it.


      The differences are:
      1. I go unconscious at zero, you don't
      2. My numbers vary with the threat of the attack, yours don't
      3. You have to roll on the tables every time (but the HP spent protects you on the tables), I only have to when the HP is depleted completely
      3. Overall the numbers and tables are slightly different
      4. [Not sure if this is a difference but in 5e there are lots of ways to straight up die, bypassing these tables - such as exhaustion, extreme hp loss, or certain spells]

    A Descriptive Damage Hack for Dungeon World/World of Dungeons

      \n I'll get back to this in more detail, but I read the 5E online (SRD-type) rules, and they do seem to align with your "vision" fairly well. But there's nothing there about injury - what's that like? There's a table for various injury types?

      \n I wrote about this upthread but to put it all in one place: it's an optional rule in the DMG page 272. The table is not open source but here it is:\n
      1 Lose an Eye.
      You have disadvantage on Wisdom (Perception) checks that rely on sight and on ranged attack rolls. Magic such as the regenerate spell can restore the lost eye. If you have no eyes left after sustaining this injury, you're blinded.

      2 Lose an Arm or a Hand. You can no longer hold anything with two hands, and you can hold only a single object at a time. Magic such as the regenerate spell can restore the lost appendage.

      3 Lose a Foot or Leg. Your speed on foot is halved, and you must use a cane or crutch to move unless you have a peg leg or other prosthesis. You fall prone after using the Dash action. You have disadvantage on Dexterity checks made to balance. Magic such as the regenerate spell can restore the lost appendage.

      4 Limp. Your speed on foot is reduced by 5 feet. You must make a DC 10 Dexterity saving throw after using the Dash action. If you fail the save, you fall prone. Magical healing removes the limp.

      5-7 Internal Injury. Whenever you attempt an action in combat, you must make a DC 15 Constitution saving throw. On a failed save, you lose your action and can't use reactions until the start of your next turn. The injury heals if you receive magical healing or if you spend ten days doing nothing but resting.

      8-10 Broken Ribs. This has the same effect as Internal Injury above, except that the save DC is 10.

      11-13 Horrible Scar. You are disfigured to the extent that the wound can't be easily concealed. You have disadvantage on Charisma (Persuasion) checks and advantage on Charisma (Intimidation) checks. Magical healing of 6th level or higher, such as heal and regenerate, removes the scar.

      14-16 Festering Wound. Your hit point maximum is reduced by 1 every 24 hours the wound persists. If your hit point maximum drops to 0, you die. The wound heals if you receive magical healing. Alternatively, someone can tend to the wound and make a DC 15 Wisdom (Medicine) check once every 24 hours. After ten successes, the wound heals.

      17-20 Minor Scar. The scar doesn't have any adverse effect. Magical healing of 6th level or higher, such as heal and regenerate, removes the scar.
      \nOr you can use any other hit location method. What I like about this version of the table is that it explicitly says how to fix the injury, and that it ranges from pretty mild to pretty bad. (Of course you can also die outright on the death saves before even getting to this table.)

      It also gives three options for how to use this:
      \u2022 Any crit [My comment this could be a way for you to get the action movie style you want: soldiering on even after like losing a foot or something]
      \u2022 When the character drops to 0
      \u2022 When the player rolls 4 or lower on a death save [rephrased, their phrasing is more convoluted]

      [My comment: Of course if you combine two or more of these, you get even more injuries. Crits AND zeros AND super low death save rolls.]

      It also goes on give an alternative option: to use the same table but only use the bolded part and then not put a game effect in there, to just allow players to use it for insp fishing a la Fate consequences.

      It's then followed by another optional rule I didn't notice until today which makes it easier for characters to die from big hits and introduces a "system shock" roll table. It's interesting, if you take a certain amount of HP loss in one sweep you roll on this table and some of the effects include falling unconscious or getting stunned. But, it clashes with my vision of what HP is, and the characters in my game die easily enough.
      \n

      \n In my games, and we use the drop-to-zero method, crits have been more common than drop-to-zero and obv super low death saves only can happen after someone has already dropped to zero, so they're per definition rarer. Not strictly, because someone could then sustain multiple lingering injuries.
      Another reason I've chosen the drop-to-zero option is that it's something you can roll if the character survives. No need to do it if they're already dead.
      But the crit version I see a lot of appeal, and also combining versions.

      The sort of fantasy I'm after is "it's all fun and games until someone gets to zero", but as you can see there are some options there.

      \n
      \n So, how does it work? (And those are pretty grim! Wow!)

      When you reach 0 hit points, what happens next? Do you roll a save, and either die or end up horribly injured? The basic rules say that you die if you fail three death saves after reaching 0 hit points; do you get a horrible injury every time you reach 0 hit points (under your rules), or is it more convoluted than that?

      I don't like that there's no relationship between *how* you lost your last hit points and the injury you receive, but that's pretty typical for how D&D rules tend to work, and it's probably functional enough most of the time.

      I do like how it makes falling to 0 hp very scary (and consequential) but effectively relatively toothless if you have access to magical healing.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\n2097 \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n How it works:
      Get to 0.
      Roll death saves. It's a convoluted procedure that involves getting three fails or three successes with lots of exceptions and special weirdness but typically you either die then and there, or are unconscious for 1d4 hours.

      If you don't die, we roll on the table. (Again, there are other options but that's how I do it.)
      If you do die then we don't roll on the table.\n
      I don't like that there's no relationship between *how* you lost your last hit points and the injury you receive, but that's pretty typical for how D&D rules tend to work, and it's probably functional enough most of the time.
      \nYeah, a couple of dissonant ideas come up and the group weave it together into a coherent narrative. Like "Your foot got burned off in the fireball" or "Your face is completely disfigured with bubbles from the venom spray".\n
      I do like how it makes falling to 0 hp very scary (and consequential) but effectively relatively toothless if you have access to magical healing.
      \nWhat has happened is that characters have died because they were so worn down with lost feet and broken ribs that the next fight, before they even had magical healing, were more dangerous.

      And to heal some of this stuff we're talking level 11 characters.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Cool, that seems pretty workable. It's also a good match for your vision of hit points as "dramatic immunity in a given fight", which recovers quickly and isn't strictly related to your state of injury.

      It does, to my mind, still have the usual issues of D&D hit points, which is that they simply break down any time we zoom in on what's happening enough to discuss the details of what's happening. The earlier "presidential assassination" is one example; here's another:

      You're fighting a monster, and one of your hands is stuck to one of those magically-sticky D&D spiderwebs. You have your sword in your other hand, fending off the beast. However, a friend (or perhaps a sadistic enemy!) comes by and says they chop at your arm with their sword. They're trying to cut off your hand, so you'll be free from the web.

      As soon as we "zoom in" on the fiction to that kind of degree, traditional D&D-style hit points become incredibly clumsy and they get in our way. If the idea is that no one gets hurt until they lose all their hit points, how do we handle this? Sure, there's lots of ways we can do it, but not by following the existing rules - we have to break away from them.

      Some people are very good at doing that; they make a ruling on the spot and we move on. (And many versions of D&D have some add-on rules for "called shots" or "critical injuries" to paste over this kind of cludge.)

      A similar issue is walking up behind someone and trying to knock them out by hitting them over the head. I've yet to see a D&D rule for this kind of thing which actually works, and, even if it does, it's going to be an exception-based rule. Hit points just won't cut it.

      To me, it's awkward game design if it forces you to do that (i.e. make rulings on the spot, or pull in optional rules, because the basic rules can't handle that situation). I prefer playing a game where that kind of conundrum doesn't come up in the first place.

      Hit points work just great when all you're doing is narrating abstract actions - "I'm attacking so and so", "A new encounter begins here", "You two are attacking the ogre; she is casting a spell".

      For a game which focuses in more on the specifics of the imagined space, however, things can get pretty difficult. Since Dungeon World-type games seek to deliver that kind of experience, I feel they need rules which interact with those fictional details in a productive way, so that our narrations and our character choices matter.




      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
