      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      You can describe the attacks in detail in a way that's much freeer than freeform? How?
      And what is the narrative/descriptive "input" to the damage rolls?
      \nWhen I say it's "freer", I don't mean that there's more liberty in narration. Rather, what I mean is that you don't have to worry about the ramifications. You can safely narrate that crocodile bite, and the players aren't going to go, "Hey, no! Take that back!" It's a safety net that allows you to feel relaxed narrating freely.

      The narrative/descriptive input is all the fictional detail of the situation. We can't know what "it hampers you" can even mean without describing the attack - well, where did you get hit? How bad was it? Etc. The whole thing operates on description and narration instead of numbers, just like other things in *World resolution.\n

      1. Let's say the GM describes the crocodile chomping hard on with all its might on the arm, tearing it off. Now roll resist harm. What happens on a 10+ result?
      \nThe main problem here is that the GM narrated the arm being torn off. That's wrong (just like it would be in D&D). Instead, the GM narrates, say, the crocodile's jaw closing around the arm (and perhaps makes it clear that it's trying to tear it off, as crocodiles like to do).

      On a 10+, the player got pretty lucky - which we can narrate as pulling the arm out of the crocodile's maw before too much damage is done, for instance.

      That's pretty key here: we must envision an outcome where the character got pretty lucky - the very best - or nearly the very best - outcome which is plausible in this scenario.

      The player must choose to suffer one effect. That could be shock from the pain (you "don't get to react" to this - I would personally describe this as the crocodile throwing the PC by the arm, and them landing somewhere painfully, but the arm is miraculously still attached and not too badly hurt), an injury which hampers him (in this case, it's pretty clear the arm isn't going to work too well!), or problems which surface after the fight (perhaps bleeding or torn ligaments). In all these cases, we just ask more questions about the fiction until we narrow it down to something everyone feels is "right", giving us a very detailed perception of the events, and keeping our heads "in the fiction".

      For example, let's say I chose the best options for winning the fight: I get to react right away or maintain my position, and I'm not hampered by the injury. What this means is that there will be consequences to deal with later - I got lucky, but I didn't get *that* lucky.

      This establishes some creative constraints on how we imagine this. Since the injury doesn't hamper me, clearly the arm is basically OK - we can't say it's been torn off or anything like that.

      So, we play out the fight, and afterwards, when the adrenaline wears off, I say, "Hey, GM, so now I take a look at my injuries. How bad does it look?"

      This is another perfect example of why all the earlier narration really matters. The GM has to come up with something which fits with everything that has been described so far: she might say that there are gouges where the crocodile's teeth cut into the flesh as I was thrown against the wall, deep enough to bleed freely. Now we have a situation to deal with: we have to take care of my injury, stop the bleeding, etc.

      Same goes for your second question/example:

      The rules don't give us clear answers; instead they create narrative constraints and then encourage us to talk to each other. They shape the conversation of play and get us all to imagine vividly what's going on and find ways to communicate with each other.

      In this case, the rules don't say for sure what happened; what they do instead is require us to find a description which is consistent with a heavy blow to the head but doesn't have immediate consequences. (My first thought is a concussion!)

      Like all RPGs, this is what we do: we build a plausible fiction together. One group might answer this very differently from another - if they have a more "cinematic" sensibility, they could describe things very differently. If we're imagining the game as a cartoon, we could even describe the mace or rock or hammer breaking on the character's head!\n

      3. Let's say the assassin that cannot miss, uses a magical bullet, laced with strong poison for the weak, and a rifle that never ever misses, shoots the local orc president princess (a PC) right in the head. Now roll resist harm. What happens on a 10+ result?
      \nSame here: we need to create a description which is consistent with our constraints. We know that the bullet has hit the PC in the head, and we know that she "got lucky" somehow (assuming the player chose that option). What does that mean?

      I can imagine us describing the PC experiencing a glancing shot to the head, her thick skull or her tiara turning away the bullet (or at least turning it away from vital areas). Maybe an ear got torn off!

      I personally know someone who took a bullet through the head (including through the brain, and coming out the other side!) and was back at work a few weeks later, without any obvious side effects. So nature is stranger than fiction in this case!

      But you have to go with something that is plausible to your group - I can imagine someone who's never heard of such a thing would find it incredible. I firmly believe in the variability of injury, though: there was a case of a woman falling from 15,000 feet without a parachute and surviving the fall, and others have died falling just a few feet. Physical harm is unpredictable! This hack reflects that.\n

      4. Let's say the space suit is all that protects you from the mindflayer-infested vacuum of aetherspace. If the air gets sucked out you're a goner. And someone chops a scimitar at your arm. Now roll resist harm. What happens on a 10+ result?
      \nI would rule that the space suit is torn - surely it's not scimitar-proof! If it IS at least potentially scimitar-proof, then I'd make a soft move to that effect and play to see what happens. This is a *World question, not a "descriptive harm" hack question. Resist harm, in my mind, anyway, is just about damage to the character, not incidental stuff like clothing or the window behind them.

      But perhaps we'd give the player another resist harm roll, to see how the vacuum of space affects them! Now it's getting trickier! Again, a detailed fiction and keeping things plausible is the way to go. "You got lucky" might give you a little longer to live - some air trapped in your suit, or whatever. If we can't see a way how that could save you long-term though, you're a goner. The GM makes some moves to that effect, and we follow the consequences/implications to their natural conclusion.
      \n

      6. Let's say some living brains are flying around, zapping people with paralyzation rays, and someone gets zapped, flubbing their defy danger roll. They roll a 10+ result on resist harm. What happens?
      \nWell, again, they would choose one option to suffer. Either they're temporarily paralyzed but recover quickly, or vice-versa (they're fine now, but only for a few moments). If they choose a temporary setback (they can't react or maintain their position), clearly the paralysis ray affected them for a moment, but they're not actually paralyzed - it just messed them up for a second. It's just like making your saving throw - you can shrug it off.\n

      7. Let's say four guards stuck in a web spell are chopping at a PC soldier with halberds, well within reach. The soldier rolls a 7-9 on hack & slash. What happens?
      \nThis is a Dungeon World (or whatever!) thing, nothing to do with the hack. If on a 7-9 people tend to get hit, I'd describe something plausible, like several halberds hitting the character. Then we describe in more detail and roll and select options, as for all the other examples. Getting hit with a couple of halberds is not a special case in any way, except that it allows the GM to narrate more potential problems. Again, it's all about the narrative detail. For instance, I could say that one halberd hit you in the arm and another in the leg. Your resist harm roll will now play with both of these things - if the "injury" hampers you, then both your arm and your leg have been pretty badly hurt.

      \n Now, here's where your help could be useful:

      Can you think of any simple solutions to the mathematical breakpoint issues involved in spending points from a pool on a roll?

      So far, we've discussed adding *dice* to the pool (and keeping the best two), which means each subsequent hit point spent has much less impact. That should incentivize people to spend fewer points, shouldn't it?

      I also mentioned the idea of having a chart of bonuses, each of which can only be used once. That takes the math out of it to a certain extent - you'll probably use the best ones at first, and then so on down the line. You can't spend +4/+4/+0, because we can design a chart which doesn't allow that option.

      Can you think of any others?

      This seems like a fairly important issue, since it applies to ANY game which involves spending points for a bonus on a roll (which is a LOT of games!). Clever solutions would be a very popular thing for designers to look at!

      \n First of all, the help:
      I've already suggested lampshading it. To put in "it's best to spend the full four, if you're going to spend anything" in the text. People could still choose to spend 1, 2 or 3, but the design would have covered its back. Then playtest with that suggestion.

      My other suggestions you've already seen, to make the hp cost proportional to the danger of the attack (as per D&D).

      \n Hmmm. Just thinking out loud, not sure yet in terms of conclusions:

      Spending 4 or 0 makes the move relatively uninteresting (the sweet spot for AW moves is rather in the +1-3 area in this case). Although worth thinking about it!

      Making the hp cost proportional to the danger of the attack might be problematic, since it effectively means more dangerous attacks are safer (more chance of failure on a "weak" attack, that's weird to me).

      \n Second of all, the execution/effect quantum state, my impression:

      In the hack, you have to freeze time at just the right moment: with just a few drops of EE that happen for sure. Actually well past execution and a little bit into effect.
      For example, the crocodile tearing the arm off. You ruled that the teeth definitely touched the skin but the arm wasn't torn off yet -- time stops and you have to immediately do the resist harm roll to find out.

      Finding the exact right moment is difficult. You could say that the teeth have penetrated the skin before the time freeze. You could say that the arm has been a little torn before the time freeze. Since everything that happens before the RH roll happens, it's very important what moment you choose. I do not like having such responsibility as DM.
      Not only that, but communicating exactly which frame we froze at also seems very very difficult in my eyes, even though it's probably a learnable skill. We're looking at a lot of "Well, I thought X happened, didn't it?"

      Freeze the time too soon and RH is effectively just a "second parachute" after defy danger / hack&slash. Freeze the time too late and RH can't help you. And there is no clear stopping point, we're well into effect but not too far.

      This difficulty I consider similar to the original crocodile story where there was too much power in my hands as DM and the players pushed back.

      In D&D the breakpoint is late in the execution rather than early in the effect. We don't have to wait to the blade starts to go into the neck, the teeth start to touch the skin. And since the effect is the much more crucial part, being able to put the breakpoint anywhere in the execution is great, much easier calls, much fewer disagreements.
      Also in D&D, we don't have to freeze time, we know whether or not the effect took you out, which is the salient part, and then we can put the rest of the effect in the cat box and later see "Oh, looks like it tore your arm off my friend" or we can roll it out right now. It gives us tremendous flexibility in how to run it / describe it while giving us a rigidity and clarity of results + interesting fights with a lot of back and forth trying to tire each other out so we can land that final blow.

      This rigidity comes at the significant cost of a too high proportion of "left facing arrows". (I know we're not allowed to use too technical lingo on S-G so in short): the mechanics influence the narration to a greater amount than vice versa, and ideally we'd want mechanics that rely on narration, incorporate narration, and solidify narration.

      So I definitely see the reason for wanting a hack such as this. Unfortunately I don't think this one is
      - well-positioned in the IIEE cycle enough to be clear
      - significantly incorporating the narration [i.e. it has even fewer right-facing arrows than D&D while having significantly fewer left-facing ones -- and more rolls] -- no matter what happens, it's the same old resist harm roll with the same old outcomes from it
      - tactically interesting
      - giving the GM any guidance on the amount and level of threats they can bring to bear
      - different enough from just a second and third layer of defy danger
      - precise enough in the types of harm and effects of that harm needed at the game table
      - giving the GM or group any clarity about how much of the effect happens regardless of the RH roll

      \n Sandra,

      Well, it seems I have somehow created a real vehicle for your game-critic hatred! I'm sorry about that - certainly there was no offense intended here. I just wrote some rules that *I* think would be fun to play. I still think so. If you don't, it's no skin off my back!

      I'm also not sure why you feel you are being tricked or misled - the mithril thing certainly wasn't a "rhetorical trap" or some such. It was just an example of something you *could* do with the rules. It's not to my taste, personally, but I could see people with different tastes enjoying it. No big deal.

      I've been trying to communicate with in good faith and as clearly as I can (keeping in mind this isn't my day job or anything; I'm not working with an editor or giving this more than a few minutes' thought here and there).

      I think that you are reading too much into the difficulties of "freezing time". It's no different from, well, basically everything we do when we roleplay, ever.

      Just like D&D attacks must be carefully narrated to be somewhere in the Initiation-Execution area (and retconned if you go too far, without anyone even noticing!), here we must make sure not to include too much Effect, or be willing to retcon it if we slip. Same with saving throws, or contest rolls or Diplomacy checks. No big deal; again, if we manage to do it in every other aspect of gaming, I'm optimistic it can't be *that* hard. (And particularly in *World games, we have to juggle that kind of thing *all the time*. I suppose - indeed, I *know* - some people do find that challenging and therefore don't play *World games. Well, this hack's not for them. No problem - they wouldn't be interested in it in the first place.)

      As another example, it's exactly like the way "Taking the Blow" works in Dogs in the Vineyard, and that seems to work pretty well - you accept Execution but stop at Effect.

      As for the "GM power" issues, it may help you to see it from my perspective. You see, I have absolutely no problem coming up with colourful narration and building on it. I don't need to make four attack rolls to be able to say that someone swings three times before finally connecting. That's the *fun* part of roleplaying, for me. Vivid imagination and all that.

      What I find difficult and taxing is deciding on the effects of that narration. Ok, the ogre hit Jelly in the gut... that's gotta hurt. No problem so far. But now what happens? Can she get up and shake it off? Will it affect her ability to fight? Will it still hurt *after* the fight?

      That stuff... I feel like it crosses a line for me. I don't want to be the one to say, "Yeah, sorry, actually you can't move at all, and you won't be able to for hours yet," purely on a whim. I'd like the system to help me here.

      So, in this hack, that's precisely what happens: we have a variety of moves (from our favourite *World game) to create and adjudicate some action scenes, and then, when someone gets hurt, I just have to say what it looks like "on-screen" and then we roll the dice. The player then chooses if they want short-term problems or long-term problems, and the results give us plenty of feedback on how to describe what happens next.

      It's exactly like the way all the other *World moves work - a clear fictional trigger, and then a list of options which we use as a skeleton to hang further descriptions from. This kind of thing, in my experience, creates really cool and really vivid fiction, and really fast. It supports the kind of play I like, and makes it easy.

      Here's one area where I feel you're entirely wrong, incidentally:

      "- significantly incorporating the narration [i.e. it has even fewer right-facing arrows than D&D while having significantly fewer left-facing ones -- and more rolls] -- no matter what happens, it's the same old resist harm roll with the same old outcomes from it"

      Since all the outcomes are based on preceding narration and descriptions, every single "resist harm" roll's outcomes are new and fresh and different. *Everything* in this hack proceeds from left to right and then back. The clouds are involved in every step of the way, because the options themselves are meaningless without the fiction. "It doesn't hamper you" means nothing without a narration of the nature of the injury, just like "you take definite hold of it" on a seize by force tells us nothing without the preceding narrations and the fictional positioning. Just like we have to position ourselves to make "take definite hold of it" an option for your character, here we have to create compelling fiction to make "your arm doesn't work anymore" plausible.

      There's something VERY different about the way you and I are imagining it, clearly, because we apparently have completely opposite views of how it plays out. Unfortunately, that's something we'll never be able to resolve unless we actually play together and try some different variations. (I'd be up for a brief "mini-playtest", if you're interested, though scheduling might be disastrous! I've done a few over Skype with other mechanics and they've been pretty fruitful.)

      I'm definitely open to discussing the details, as well as possible improvements. It would be pretty interesting to see how YOU would try to achieve my goals given your vision of how such rules operate in play. If you have better ideas, I'd love to see them. Again, this is basically a first draft on my part, not the final stage of an artistic manifesto after years of failed prototypes. I'd love to see other peoples' brainstorms on this topic.

      As for the math of point-spending, I'm somewhat on the fence. Ideally, you *could* occasionally spend so many points that you're guaranteed to survive, but the system would be set up so that those opportunities would be *very rare*. Most rolls, ideally, would fall into the +1 to +3 or +4 range, with occasional zeroes and much higher numbers. (I can see a +6 or +7 on occasion being a good thing.)

      This suggests that some kind of increasing costs would be handy, which is why I'm somewhat tempted by the idea of diminishing returns, like the dice pool idea floated earlier. (Add one die per hit point spent, roll and keep the best two.) Do you still feel that's not a good idea? How would you analyze that, from a mathematical perspective? You know now what kind of behaviour I would like to see; is there a fairly simple model which would create that kind of behaviour?

      \n First of all, before I answer the rest\u2026
      I don't wish to be so hateful
      I want peace and respect
      I think part of the problem, and I introduced this, was that we were discussing two different systems (the hack and the vision), comparing and contrasting them, sure, but also sometimes arguing about both of them, a conflict on two fronts.
      And since I know this hack is an alpha thing I want to make it better.

      Secondly, I am genuinely confused about where to draw the line in e.g. the arm tear case. The difference between stopping somewhere in the execution is that it's just sword clanging at that point (all of SCA just gasped in horror at me). Stopping somewhere in the effect, now\u2026 you've emphasized a couple of times that it's important that there's some effect before you go to RH. Just how much of that effect there is seems to me to be very impactful, very hard to choose and very un-assisted by the system.
      That is a huge crux for me.

      Going to the stomach punch. Perfect example. For me, even saying it's hurting, goes over one line. And then the long-term consequences goes over another.
      Unfortunately, as far as I understand it wrt injuries, you kinda need to see the big impact in order to determine the original pain. That croco arm? Shredded, torn off or just grazed?


      To use a metaphor, and now I'm going to talk about BP as literally your body points rather than your luck/fatigue, which I'll call HP: you know the crocodile has hit. It's going to eat up at least one BP, then we'll make a roll to see if it's any more. But the GM has to decide how many BP the crocodile eats up. Just one, or ten, twenty? And there is always some effect, what happens happens, the stomach got punched, the head got shot. But what that effect is is in the hand of the GM.

      However, I'm thinking that you could have some clearly delineated lines here. DD/HS failed: you get this-and-that well defined consequence [future harm badness], + a RH roll. That failed too, then you get this-and-than well defined consequence [real harm].

      Defining these lines of consequences in a way that's both consistent and general looks like a significant challenge to me, though. In BP terms, it's like saying "the crocodile always eats 3 on hit, roll to see if there's more". But in narrative terms? That's difficult.

      As far as the dice pool thing.
      Any mechanic where you put in a certain amount of currency A to affect randomness, and then get different effects in another currency B based on the randomness but you still lose your A, is going to have breakpoints. (A and B can be the same, or like in this case different where A is your numerical hp pool and B is your characters well-being in narrative terms.) Already we have a showstopper here.

      If the randomness has few other parameters, which this has with it's fixed difficulty numbers and vaguely defined outcomes, those breakpoints are going to be much fewer and firmer.

      The effect of A on the randomness being non-linear, such as dice pools and bell curves, are also contributing to this.

      We could get on IRC to playtest and I could do the math (if I could somehow move my desk, it's not OK to work in the window on hot days like this).

      Not necessarily today though, I gather it's quite late over there!

      \n I guess this is also a genre thing. People taking a couple serious blows and gashes and fighting on -- that's what you want, right?
      Whereas what I wanted was the more swashbuckly/chanbara like one-hit-one-kill, but a lot of effort needed to land that one blow.

      What neither of us wants is cartoony taking blow after blow and not be affected -- that's how I think many people played D&D.

      I think I could help coming up with a "vision" or a couple of extra layers on DW or D&D that make it fit the genre you want rather than the genre I want.

      But I think you could do it better yourself, if we take a step back and look at the adjudication timelines clearly (i.e. make it strict and clear exactly when the dice come in and what we know before that) and maybe rethink the variable point spend idea.

      \n Mathed out the dice pool method for weights up to 10 10 0 (keeping the 10+ weight at a constant 0 since 13+ isn't possible, right?) and I'm working on bigger weighs too (dice pool stuff is sloooo). There's probably a max where it doesn't make sense to spend more.

      So far, it's looking pretty fine-grained:
      ((0 0 0) (0 0))
      ((3 0 0) (1 1.21))
      ((4 3 0) (2 2.7))
      ((5 5 0) (3 3.49))
      ((7 7 0) (4 4.14))

      The left triplets are the weights ("pain"-cost of 6-, 7-9 and 10+) and the right pairs are the ideal point spend and the average amount of wasted points with that spend, which is to be as low as possible.

      \n
      \n
      \n Thanks, Sandra!

      You are quite right about the genre. I suppose that, for the "chanbara" style of fighting, you almost don't really need a damage system, do you? You can just call it "defeat" (and maybe include something about death or survival, for the PCs), and you're done.

      My usual issue with "chanbara" fighting is that it should be all about positioning and jockeying for advantage. Very few games manage that - in fact, none that I've played. And I certainly wouldn't want the D&D approach, where heavy armour and heavy weapons are the main factor in who wins. I'd love to see a game that does it well, though.

      For my vision of D&D, however, it's not like that at all. There's injuries and badass heroes who can push the pain away and do what needs to be done.


      1. I'd love to hear your suggestions on improving the design (or even an entirely different design!). It's pretty interesting stuff, which I wish games would explore more. (Also, I assume you've seen my other harm hacks, like the original one for Apocalypse World?)


      2. I can write out the procedure for using this hack in more concrete terms, if that helps.

      Steps:

      a) We determine (however we do) that a PC has been hit. (e.g. a failed roll)

      b) The MC describes what the attack *looks like*, including where and how it strikes the PC. (Just like drawing a panel in a comic book, of the attacker delivering the blow.) But she does not describe any of the effects of the injury on the PC himself.

      c) It's nice - but not necessary - if the MC describes some sensory details, like sounds or physical sensations, in order to paint a fairly complete picture.

      Example: "As you're running towards the door, the paralysis ray sweeps across and hits your left leg just above the knee. There is a smell like burning ozone, and a nearly overwhelming tingling - like sparks running through your nerves - shoot up and down your leg."

      d) If this is a game style where objectivity and clarity are important, the MC can say what exactly the attack threatens to do (e.g. "this crocodile is strong enough that it could tear your arm off!", or "it feels like the paralysis effect is spreading through your entire body; you could be completely immobile within moments").

      e) The PC gives in to the moment (an incentive here would be nice, like marking experience, or recovering some hit points*), or rolls the resist harm move.

      f) If the PC gives in or rolls a miss, the MC describes the full extent of the injury, as befits the earlier narration. The PC is paralyzed or has their arm torn off, in other words.

      g) If the PC rolled a hit, the MC describes the injury, but obeys the constraints imposed by the move. If the PC maintains their position or momentum, the blow was light (or at least light enough that they could shrug it off for now); if the injury doesn't hamper them, there can't be any obvious damage to bones, muscles, tendons; if they "got lucky", there can't be any damage which would require serious care after the fact.

      The MC is free to make it as scary and colourful as she wishes, so long as she obeys those constraints.

      To use the paralysis ray example:

      - On a miss: "You fall to the ground, your limbs jerking spasmodically."

      - On a hit, there are many different possibilities, depending on the roll and the options chosen. Let'say player chooses that they got lucky and that they can maintain their momentum: "You grit your teeth and keep running, pushing your way through the door. When you get to the other side, though, your leg gives out - you won't be running much further until it wears off."

      And so on.


      *: I'm actually kind of liking this idea. Maybe the PC rolls their hit die and adds that to their hit points. This would make resilient Fighter types more likely to put themselves in the way of danger and enable dramatic "He went down over there, but now he's rising from the rubble and he looks pissed!" moments.

      Sidenote: It's also totally fine to include any other details you like, so long as you can imagine the character surviving them. For instance, I could say, "There is a gunshot, deafening in the small room, and you look down, in shock, to see blood spreading out on your white shirt."

      I don't think you have to be all that careful in your descriptions, so long as whatever you narrate is something we all think it's plausible for the character to ignore for now. Tearing off an arm is clearly out, but cutting off the shirt and finding that it looked scary because of the blood but in fact is just a shallow graze which will easily heal by itself is very in-genre here.


      3. I'd love it if you could explain the math in more detail. I'm not sure how you're setting or generating any of these numbers (well, mostly the ones in the left column, I think I understand the right column). Are they arbitrary breakpoints you're choosing to test, or are they generated by some kind of formula?

      Thanks!

      \n I don't have a dog in this fight (indeed, I've only kind of skimmed the thread), but a few observations:

      a) when you make survival dependent on a dwindling pool of resources (even if its grooved spends like Gumshoe or the alpha version of this hack), it is important to remember that you are now centralizing the concept of being worn down, husbanding resources, trying to find the last ounce of ability (and also creative ways to avoid having to do just that.) This may seem obvious, but for example IMHO Gumshoe tends to not realize how central this experience has become. (And as a counter example, D&D tends to not result in this experience as HP have no effect before they are gone and don't limit your ability to do stuff like Gumshoe pools do.)

      b) if your goal is to allow the best spend, but also shake it up so that other spends become rational, then I think the easiest solution is to make the cost non-linear. Perhaps something like:

      +1 for 1 HP
      +2 for 3 HP
      +3 for 5 HP
      +4 for 7 HP

      ...or other scales; maybe Sandra can figure 'em out. (I can't read matrix math, so I need to wait for translation.)

      FWIW, Paul, I think the original AW hack you did avoided some of these issues by tiering how bad the potential damage was; maybe it's too gritty for DW, but I wonder if some of Sandra's feeling that results don't make sense might be alleviated by a division along those lines.

      \n The issue with using a "reliable" point spend is that we effectively make the random part of the process obsolete. Why bother rolling a move, if you're going to spend points to buy success anyway? It would be more direct to just spend a random number of points (as it works in D&D).

      I could see some kind of variation like this, though (I don't like it as much, but I bet some people would):

      Keep the categories of the "resist harm" move, but remove the roll. Instead, on a hit the MC declares one of the following:

      * The blow is minor - a bruising, broken skin, superficial damage. (1)
      * The blow is serious - wounding, broken bones, bleeding, etc. (2)
      * The blow is traumatic, deadly - serious bodily damage which isn't possible to heal by natural means. (3)

      Now, the player may spend that number of hit points for each option from the "resist harm" list they wish to "buy".

      I get hit with a serious blow, I can "buy" "you got lucky" for 2 points, or "you gut lucky" + "you can react immediately" for 4 points, for instance.

      It's not to my tastes (I like more unpredictability in my injuries, and it makes it harder for the MC to make those calls, as well, because they have strong mechanical ramifications), but, again, some people might dig those features.

      \n Question 2: thanks for writing it out in detail. That explanation leaves the space suit unexplained (which surprised me, I would've guessed it was one of the easier ones) but answers a lot of the rest. Thanks.

      Question 1.
      This goes against my grain since I'm not really into movies like Die Hard and stuff like that where they got glass in the feet and stuff, I'm weirdly enough more squeamish about that than I am about a good old arm chop!

      But.

      How often, approximately, do you want the wounds to
      1. Be a life-ending wound
      2. Be a fight-ending wound
      3. Be a real wound but they can still fight on
      4. Not be a real wound?

      I would design a die roll with those four approximate thresholds.
      Let's say I want all four of them in equal measure (I don't think that's gonna be very good, but for ease of explanation let's start with that). So a 1d4 roll on that table.

      Then I would put in that roll instead of the normal damage roll in DW.

      For example.

      Crocodile fightin time!
      But you roll a 6- vs old tick tock!
      Roll a d4. Oh, you rolled a 3. The arm can be torn off but you can still be in the fight.

      Or if you had rolled a 2, the arm would be torn off but you would also be unconscious.
      Or if you had rolled a 1 it would just be a little ouch, a minor scar that you can cover up with a t-shirt

      Or, Ambrose Bierce style knight fight in a dark house!
      But you roll a 6- vs old knifey!
      Roll a d4. On a 4, you got killed!
      on a 3, you fainted from the knife threats and the darkness
      on a 2, you got grazed, it's gonna leave a mark, but the fight goes on
      on a 1, your shirt got torn? or a surface scratch a little blood but something that'll heal in a day or so?

      (You can see me still struggling with the "stomach punch" example. It's so contrary to my whole thinking and intuition that something can hurt but not really be an injury)


      Now, starting with this, there are two things left to do:
      1. Tweak the percentages, 25% equal chance of each is probably not what you want. Remember though that these only happen on a Deal Harm hard DM move.
      2. Put in some agency if you want it. Like some sorta limited resource to use up. I kinda... I kinda don't like that sorta econ in my games? Bad memories of stalled and grindy Fate games 'cause everyone's at zero FP come up... (Obv you'd want to set the percentages with whatever agency mechanic you put in there in mind.)

      But regards to the percentages, let's say that since 2d6 is readily at hand, you want a 3- to be real death (around 8-9%), 6- to be a fight ending wound as you go unconscious / and or taken-out, crying, sick, 7-9 to be a real wound but they can still fight on, and 10+ to be the "oh it only hurt a bit but that's fine" result that I'm still having a hard time grokking.
      This replaces the 1d4 thing.


      Or, and this is my preference, instead of a random roll each time, there's this thing that gets eaten up, this clock of destiny that your luck is running out until you can reset it with a good rest.
      HP!!!
      Just put in a couple of thresholds:
      At HP X you are dead, at HP Y you are taken-out but living, at HP Z you are hurt for real, otherwise you're just slightly hurt!

      For example, HP over your half is luck and sweat, HP below your half is blood and meat.

      In "my vision", it's all fun and games (sorry for the repeated catch phrase, it's getting tiring I bet), but in this tweaked version it's only half fun and games. You can't brush it off once you're down past the threshold. Don't worry player's we're not changing anything in our home campaign!

      (Essentially this is a model that has been used many times. 4e, WotC Star Wars, Fate (kinda)...)

      \n Sandra,

      The reason the spacesuit is unexplained it because I think it's not a feature of the "harm mechanics", but, rather, of the *World "conversation". The suit gets punctured a) when it's fictionally appropriate, and when b) it's the MC's turn to make a move. If it's my game, the moment you're hit by anything which could plausibly damage your suit, it's torn. However, unless it's the main focus of the action (e.g. an enemy is deliberately trying to tear your suit, that's what we're rolling about, and you failed), I would make it a soft move: Air is leaking out, and soon the pressure inside the suit will drop enough that you're a goner! If you act immediately, you might be able to do something about it, but we're talking seconds at most. What are you going to do?

      As for your suggestions, as you point out yourself, if we go that route, we're recreating every other game's "damage system".

      We try to create a simulation where we track the damage potential of the weapons being used, effects of armour, the deadliness of the blow, and the sturdiness of the target, etc, etc, and find a way to randomize that, within the parameters we like to so. I've designed dozens of these, with different goals and different spreads (including some I really like, which I wrote up for Fudge), and I can always pick up GURPS or D&D or whatever game and use their rules.

      Here, I'm very intentionally trying to create something different: it's not a "simulationist" attempt to model reality at all. I'm trying to step back from the right-facing arrow stuff, the "physics modeling" stuff, and make it all hinge on the descriptions we're making in play.

      You ask questions like "will the suit be torn", or "will the arm come off", and the answer is always the same:

      We play the game with enough fictional detail to know. Will the suit be torn? Well, it depends on how thick the suit is, who the opponents are, what weapons are being used, how we described the attacks being made, how much room there is to maneuver, and so forth. It's all about the fiction - not the numbers.

      To settle such questions, we don't look to the rules. We look at the fiction and we keep defining it until the answer is clear. Ask questions, nail down details, and eventually the reality emerges. It's a rich process, with subtleties that we enjoy and that make the game feel "real".

      In other words (as an analogy), it's less like "make an ability check to clear the trap", and more like "roleplay, telling me exactly what you're doing, to see if you can disarm the trap". That's what's interesting to me here. I want the qualitative description of the harm to really matter, rather than throwing it in as an afterthought after the roll is made.

      The 1d4 system is actually pretty good, I think, but it asks us to focus on objective measures of character capacity, just like 99% of other RPG systems. "You're taken out of the fight" is clear enough, and more than enough to know what happens next. What it *doesn't* create is a need to establish more descriptive detail - does it really matter *why* you're out of the fight? Well, not really, because if it did, we could potentially bypass it.

      What do I mean by that? The rules have just told us that you're "out of the fight" - that's settled. If I ask, "How so?", I might learn that it's because I was knocked unconscious. Ah! "But!" I say, "I have this implant in my body which sends stimulants to my cerebellum when higher brain functions shut down. That means I should be back up in moments!"

      Well no. If we allowed that, we'd be breaking the rules. So now we have to either avoid making that description in the first place, or change it until it makes sense to us. (e.g. "Ok, well, the fight is almost over. Can we just say that your implant got shorted out, as well?" "Fine.")

      What I'm aiming for is rules which work the other way around: they give us the room to agree on descriptive detail, and then make everything reference that detail. If an injury hampers you here, and you can find a way around that, congrats - it doesn't matter anymore! The injury hasn't gone away, but, like the trap you sidestepped through clever roleplaying, it is no longer relevant right now. If you make the roll to recover, great, but we don't know how long it will take - that depends on the type of injury.

      Instead of referencing the rules, we're referencing the fiction.

      Yes, that means the rules themselves must necessarily be a little vague and a little fluid. But that's what gives us the room to go to the fiction first, to make things feel "alive" and "real".

      That's the design goal here.

      Not a huge fan of that version, to be honest (we discussed this earlier in the thread). If you're just spending a random number of hit points on each roll, we might as well just "roll for damage" and skip this whole process!
      \nWhich I think is great,
      \nA perfect example:

      A very functional system, but what we really *care* about here is the number. "How many hit points do I lose?"

      We have no real interest in the description. Knowing I lost 5 hit points is all I need to know - why do we care what it looked like?

      That's fine for some purposes, but it makes it really easy to play the game without mentioning anything at all. Effectively, for that moment we're not playing an RPG but a boardgame.

      I'm looking to blur the lines a bit.

      \n
      \n Right. We need more memorable terms than "left" and "right". Sorry! (Incidentally, I think that was an issue with your "Blorf Garp" terms for categories or roleplaying. No one will ever remember them, or at least not all the time. I certainly couldn't, while writing this - as you can see for yourself. )

      I think you missed the point of my example. The point I was trying to make is that fictional details can't override the mechanical output of our system. If I can "talk my way out of" being unconscious, I'm effectively cheating. For instance, in D&D, I can't lose my last hit point but then say, "Hey, but the weapon he's using probably wouldn't kill me - so let's say I'm still alive, ok?"

      The causality works the other way (at least for everyone I've seen play D&D): if the rules establish I'm dead, *then* we figure out what that looks like fictionally. We don't say, "Ok, you take 4 damage, so you're dead..." "No, hang on, he's hitting me from below, so he probably hit my leg. I'll take the 4 hit points, of course, but I can't actually die from a punch to the leg. So I'm still alive, right?" No, we usually say something like, "Ok, fine. Let's say it got you in the stomach, then."

      And that's what I'm trying to facilitate with my hack: not to have to do that.

      In most games, if we feel that the blow to the leg shouldn't be lethal, we have to adjust for that with the numbers. ("Ummm, ok, -2 to damage." Or, perhaps, "Ok, but we'll treat it as non-lethal damage." Or whatever other thing we rule at that moment.)

      With the way I'm trying to do it, the rules never put us in that position in the first place. We can just talk! All fiction, no numbers.

      If I narrate hitting you in the lower leg with my whip, the rules will never "roll a 4" and tell us that you're dead. Instead, they tell me to narrate a nasty cut to the leg, oh, and, also, that this injury will hinder you going forward, but that it didn't knock you off-balance just now. (Things that *only* fully make sense given a description of the action. For instance, how will the injury hinder you? What activities will it hinder? There's no numbers telling us; it's all based on that description - we can imagine what a cut to the leg might hinder, and what it might not.)

      That's why it's the description ("whip to the leg") which matters here, not the numbers.

      \n Ok, so here's another attempt at descriptive damage in DW:
      DM, when you deal harm, deal out a Consequence that makes sense diegetically. That might include death, unconsciousness, wounds, broken legs, minor bruises etc.
      Player, if you don't immediately die from the consequence, note it on the character sheet. From now on you have it.

      \nWhoa, really?!? What does that look like?

      Whenever this came up in our D&D games, it would just turn into an argument until we retconned it into something which satisfied everyone.

      Your DW descriptive damage sounds ok, except it's just playing freeform, isn't it? Also, it makes hit points totally irrelevant. "Playing freeform" is always an option - and sometimes a very good option - but I don't think either of us enjoying GMing that way, right?

      \n I had this same clash of ideas when I tried to understand DW from D&D perspective, and there are still many things I never got to understand well enough. But when I got the core of the thing (like 3-4 years later after AW was out), I understood that it relied on a completely different frame of thinking to be more efficient.

      But now this hack uses an entirely different frame of thinking (and one not even too close to the common AW line of though) to be more consequent to the detail of the fiction, which is great, but it's another feeling, and so far not efficient enough. No wonder Sandra fought it so much. She is used to have a system that informs her about what is going on, to associate emotions to quantities, while you Paul (kinda like me) have problems doing that because your imagination is fighting with the system instead of letting it do it's work.

      It's the same in Sandra's case, she's fighting your hack because her imagination doesn't find much to engage. Which is what your hack is about, not putting anything there to engage so your imagination can go free. No wonder this thread has gone so far in this discussion, productive as it has been, but also frustrating for all (because man, just by reading it I got frustrated too; you both have cool ideas, but you do need to walk more in each other shoes to get why they are so cool)

      \n Ok, this is pretty interesting!

      For the record, I'm quite familiar with Sandra's playstyle (not personally, but the descriptions sound like a type of play I really enjoy), have done it a bunch, and really dig it. I'd like to think that I understand it very well. (Of course, like all things in gaming, though, you don't really know until you actually play together - but, on paper, it sounds exactly like something I really enjoy.)

      WarriorMonk, you nailed the distinction in playstyle here, I think. It's very much about building on the fiction and letting your imagination go free, with mechanical touchposts on a few important issues to keep us on the same page. That's the *World model, in my opinion. I do understand that it's hard to grasp from a D&D mindset. In D&D, we roll some dice based on established procedures (e.g. "It's your turn, make your to-hit roll"), and that leads us to imagine some stuff. ("Ooh! Sounds like you got him good!") In *World games, the fictional stuff comes first, and we engage the mechanics only in retrospect (D&D is often like this outside of combat, note).

      I find it interesting that you think this hack is different from *World mechanics - in my mind, the HP/damage mechanics in DW were the part that didn't fit (AW as well, in fact!), and so I designed this to be more in line with the rest of the system. Could you tell me more about where you think it differs?

      \n Sandra,

      It sounds like we're finding some agreement! Let me go through your points and tell you what they look like from my perspective.

      First, though, I find your explanation of "SIS overriding the rules" (two posts back, on the "knocked out by a whip" topic) fairly unconvincing. Yeah, it could happen sometimes, but it's more of a breakdown of the rules (or means they were applied at the wrong time in the first place). I don't think "OK, you're down to 0 hit points... uh, but I guess you're not dead" (ignoring death saves and such for the moment, I'm thinking of B/X D&D here) is something which happens very often in a functional D&D game.

      Like you, I'm having trouble thinking of an example. Does that mean we're in agreement there?

      Generally, D&D combat is very strictly rules-first (almost all right-facing arrows): we roll to hit, we reduce your hit points, and then, if we see they reached 0, we narrate some serious injury. Do it the other way around, and it gets in your way. If I narrated a glancing blow, does that mean we should ignore a high result on the damage roll? Um... better to start over! Roll first, THEN narrate.

      I just watched the first two episodes of TitansGrave, and it's a perfect illustration of this kind of combat: by default, it's roll the dice and, optionally, fill in some description, but the description doesn't really matter. A good GM and group can make it more interesting, but that's kind of a special skill, not a fundamental principle of the rules' design. We can play out a whole fight without saying anything about the fiction at all, except who is attacking who and with what weapon.

      I generally prefer not to go point-by-point (it makes it easier to get into arguments!), but here it would take too long to summarize. Please take this in a positive spirit, Sandra! I'm just excited to talk about games. I don't see this is a contest or a fight - in fact, I'm only engaging in it because I respect your point of view and I'm really interested in seeing this through your eyes. (There's also the possible effect of improving the rules or coming up with something new!) Those are all good things!\n
      I'll copy my list of objections and annotate it
      I don't think this one is
      - well-positioned in the IIEE cycle enough to be clear

      It's clearer now but the space suit example shows that it is well into Effect. Something happens before the roll.
      \nYou're quite right that the rules don't help us to settle the spacesuit example. But is this a problem? I'm not sure any *other* RPGs help us settle this problem, either.

      In your own example, for instance, you had to make up a new rule to cover the spacesuit situation (and it arguably conflicted with your definition of hit points - since, in your view, taking hit points away means you dodge or block or avoid something, but here it would mean you DID get hit after all).

      Couldn't you make up a new rule for spacesuit-piercing just the same in any other game, as you did in your D&D example?

      Personally, I find this clarity liberating: I described someone getting hit, so, yes, they definitely got hit. Now we can talk about the spacesuit getting punctured or not, but at least we're starting from a place of "this weapon has physically made contact with this fabric - now, does that mean it's punctured?" instead of, "Some hit points have been lost - now, what does that mean? Should we interpret that as contact or not? What do hit points really represent, anyway?" The former is a much easier conversation for me to have.

      Are there any other areas where this could cause trouble? That would be something important to look out for.

      From where I'm sitting, I don't see an overlap with Effect. Execution should cover not only swinging the weapon but also connecting with the target (otherwise it would be just Initiation!). This seems much more clear to me than the other way around. Effect covers the consequences of the action. For instance, in Vincent's examples, someone gets picked up and thrown off a building. Blocking Execution means you're still on the roof (maybe wrestling with them). Blocking Effect means you grab on to a balcony on the way down (denying the intended *effect* of having them die by hitting the pavement below). Execution means that the attempt to pick them and throw them *clearly worked*, however.

      Likewise, here Execution means the sword hit the guy's arm - now we're just haggling over the effect of the blow. If the spacesuit is flimsy, it wouldn't be very plausible for it to survive that unharmed! If it's sturdy, then probably not. But neither D&D hit points nor the resist harm hack help us with that - we need to use our judgement or make a new ruling.

      If it helps, you can think of the resist harm move as taking the place of the damage roll. They're exactly in the same place in the IIEE process, I'm pretty sure.\n

      - significantly incorporating the narration [i.e. it has even fewer right-facing arrows than D&D while having significantly fewer left-facing ones -- and more rolls] -- no matter what happens, it's the same old resist harm roll with the same old outcomes from it

      The adjudication as a whole incorporates the narration but the RH itself doesn't take the narration into account by, for example, changing any numbers.
      \nWell, the hit point numbers change. You'll spend more if the attack is more dangerous or comes at a more sensitive time, or less if you think recovery will be easy. However, I agree that's a fairly "light" kind of mechanical feedback.

      So, I agree with you here. However! This is exactly how most *World moves work. There's no numbers changing and always 'the same old outcomes' with Parley or Carouse or any other move. Why should this be different?

      If you don't like this sort of game design, that's understandable. Is there some way that this move takes the fiction into account less than Parley or Carouse, though? It seems very much the same to me.

      The way the move reacts to the fiction is not by changing numbers but by influencing your interpretation of the fiction. For instance, if the recovery move says "you recover, but it takes you extra time", that's going to be *clearly* very different for someone who was slapped in the face compared to someone who has a broken leg. \n

      - tactically interesting

      This one hasn't changed either but I can understand why it's not a high priority. As an example, the reason D&D has its cumbersome and convoluted death save procedure is so that someone can cast Healing Word on you and it's a tradeoff if they have to be quick enough.
      \nThis one I don't understand. In D&D, hit point loss has *no* tactical implications except for the loss itself (up until you hit zero, anyway). In this hack, hit point loss has no tactical implications except for the loss itself, as well.

      However, when you get hit, you have to balance your current position or action against being hampered in the fight going forward and then again versus long-term trouble. That seems pretty tactically interesting to me!

      For instance, let's say I'm trying to hold back a horde of little snarling goblin-creatures at a doorway. If I get hit and I don't choose to "maintain my position", some of them are going to get by. Is that worth taking a blow for?

      It seems pretty tactically rich to me - that was kind of the point of the design.\n

      No matter how many arm-tearing crocs you want to put in there, until there's some failed RH rolls, the arms stay on. Right? If so, then yes, the hack a pretty good idea just for that.
      \nCorrect!

      \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n

      \n

      - different enough from just a second and third layer of defy danger

      This one hasn't changed either, right?
      \nThis sounds like an interesting objection. Could you explain what you mean by this?

      Ultimately, all "defensive" or reactive moves in a *World system are variations on Defy Danger, just specialized for those exact circumstances. (e.g. look at Last Breath)

      I could see adding more variety by making it a more active thing. For instance, when you get hit, you don't just roll to resist harm - instead, you roll to DO something (effectively AFTER you got hit). And the injury is somehow worked into that. Could be as simple as:

      10+ - you can ignore the injury and do it.
      7-9 - You can do it, but you're injured, or you can go down but the injury isn't serious.
      miss - You go down from the injury.

      I don't mind that, and I like the simplicity, but it doesn't allow us to take into account the nature of the danger as much. I think it would be harder to adjudicate.

      Ideally we'd also have different moves for different things you do while injured... it could get pretty cumbersome. Worth exploring, perhaps, though! Perhaps something like:

      When you soldier through the pain and keep barreling forward... (this one would be about whether you can maintain your position or advantage)

      When you take a life-threatening injury... (this one would be about whether you stay in the fight now and whether it's going to need immediate care after the fight)

      When it looks you're a goner, but you try to hold on to life... (this one would have possible outcomes of instant death or lying on the floor, taking your last breaths)\n

      - precise enough in the types of harm and effects of that harm needed at the game table

      This one I realize is a genre thing. You want a tiny scimitar to be as dangerous (numerically) as a giant dragon tooth and that's perfectly fine for your genre, so\u2026 we can scratch it.
      \nCorrect!

      Although I suppose you could make a giant table of harm results for various types of weapons and situations... and show how they interact with the options on the resist harm roll. For instance, "it hampers you" looks like THIS if you were hit by a sword, but like THAT if you got hit by a flying boulder.

      I'm not sure that's worth the trouble, though. I rather enjoy the flexibility of dealing with just this one move.\n

      - giving the GM or group any clarity about how much of the effect happens regardless of the RH roll

      You've established that it gives some clarity so this one has been counterproven. But does it give enough clarity?
      Let's go to the stomach punch example
      Someone rolls 6- on not getting punched and 10+ on RH. What exactly happens?
      \nOk, so you've been punched in the stomach? Again, first we need to know how hard and in what position and by who, and so on. Lying flat on the ground and having a rock golem smash downward with all its strength is going to look very different from taking a punch in a boxing match from a nerdy scientist who doesn't know what he's doing.

      On a 10+, you choose two options, leaving one. So, effectively, the options are:

      * You lose your position or momentum. (If you were trying to fly the blimp into the hangar, it swerves left as you buckle at the waist. Your enemy has the initiative now, not you - maybe their next action is to take control of the wheel!)
      * You keep control, but this will hamper you. (You grit your teeth and keep control of the blimp, and you can hit back immediately.) However, the MC can use this as justification to cause trouble for you - maybe you're nauseous or your head is spinning, maybe it's a -1 ongoing until the fight is over (if you're more mechanically-minded).
      * You can ignore it completely for now, but after the fight's over, you'll have a serious stomachache. (Maybe this means you don't get to recover hit points that night because it hurts and you can't eat properly.)

      \n\nWarriorMonk \n\n\n

      \n\nJuly 2016 \n edited July 2016
      \n

      \n
      \n It's perhaps about how I understood AW and what concepts are core for me and what I totally forgot about. For me AW-like means: The GM establishes a threat, the player explains their reaction and rolls, the system tells us what happens (that is, we do what the most appropiate move says) and all players agency about the effects is to choose between the choices the system offers. The point is that there are ineludible consequences nevertheless.

      But in this hack while yes, fiction comes first, HP reduction isn't a system nor a GM imposition. Your character may suffer more detailed and consequent effects, but as a player you get to choose if or when that happens. Detailed and inventive as it gets, it feels counterintuitive trying to grasp the concept. I get stabbed. Do I have the choice to bleed or not? You are telling me I've got a say on how much do I bleed? As wonderful as that sounds, it certainly breaks my immersion.

      Compared to the usual HP concept, this last one is easier to grasp: your character sheet bleeds, mechanically speaking, and you ain't got a choice on that, which feels a bit more like reality IMHO. The system tells you when this happens and even tries (unsuccessfully) to add some detail to make look different each time. But then all we gotta do is adapt what the system gives us into whatever makes more sense to the game - which for tons of people doesn't break immersion, but stimulates their imagination instead.

      So in the end, both systems can do really cool things in vastly different ways; you need to be prepared to adjust manually both of the systems shortcomings. They produce totally different tactical practices. On the hack it's more of a resource you can more freely spend, depending on the situation which is often consequent to the fiction; where in D&D is more of a resource that is taken from you and you don't have a choice on how much nor how, which according to the system the later wouldn't probably relate very well to the fiction and needs player's help to fit.

      Think in the right frame, help things a bit and both systems work like a charm, resist to it and both contraptions will utterly destroy your immersion.
      \n

      \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2016 \n edited July 2016
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Good post. Although, with each harm hack I post, I always get this response: "You are telling me I've got a say on how much do I bleed? As wonderful as that sounds, it certainly breaks my immersion."

      Yeah, that's true. Why DO I get a say in that? I understand how that might be jarring.

      And, yet, that's how SO many AW moves work - why, in fact, most of them get you to choose something bad that happens to your character despite it being out of your control.

      When I roll a 7-9 Fortunes in AW, I choose what bad stuff is happening in my hardhold. ("Um, I guess there are reprisals from the rival warlord!") When I roll Carouse in DW, I choose that I got "ensorcelled or tricked". And so on. None of these make any sense "immersively".

      To me, that's part of the AW engine: it forces difficult choices on you. Your job as the player is to make those tough calls.

      As you say: "the system tells us what happens (that is, we do what the most appropiate move says) and all players agency about the effects is to choose between the choices the system offers."

      Isn't that exactly what's happening here? You roll, and then you've got to pick some of the choices offered - and it's probably not going to look good for you.

      If it bothers you in THIS particular case, you could just say that the player spends the hit points, but the MC chooses the options. That would work, too!

      We had a long discussion about this in the original "visceral harm" thread. Some people like it one way and some people like it the other way. (Although it's pretty arbitrary - e.g. Johnstone said he preferred the MC making the call, but then in his own hack the player decides how many points of damage are "shock" and how many are "injury", which is really the same choice again, isn't it?)

      I don't like it, personally, because, as the MC I don't ever want to choose when a PC dies. Part of this is avoiding responsibility and leaving a tough call to the system. Part of it is for the flow of play: I'd rather hand off the conversation, for a more natural sequence.

      PC does something and fails -> I narrate something nasty happening to them.
      Now they roll and select options, which I use to make a move.

      It's nicely back-and-forth.

      The other way is basically all the MC talking after the initial roll, except for the player's choice to spend hit points. I suppose that's OK, but it's less fun and less interactive to me. I like the idea that the player can make tactical decisions, too, deciding whether they want to risk severe harm now or deal with the consequences later, that kind of thing. Sometimes it leads to fun situations, like the airship captain trying to keep control of the ship and having to decide just how much punishment they're willing to take in order to carry out their mission.

      It also allows a player another avenue to express their character ("Yeah, Gorgon is badass knight who always goes for the kill, regardless of how much of a mess it leaves him later - he's rageful and reckless like that!), which I consider another bonus.

      \n
      \n\nJuly 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Yet conceptually speaking nothing in AW struck me as fail foward, as your hack suggests, even if it's the player's choice. In D&D gameplay adjustment is also easy to do.

      You want action hero feeling? give tons of HP to the players (aka, higher levels) and make HP represent how much they can avoid being seriously hit, adding colorful descriptions without too much mechanical consequences.
      You want a more "realistic simulation"? stay between the first 5 levels and never roll, but use the table as the fiction requires, letting the GM and the players choose whatever makes sense given the amount of damage and how high the attack roll was. Describe hits and misses bassed on what conforms the largest part of the character's AC.
      And you can have "let's go for whatever comes out of these freaking awesome tables" feeling too, and just try to make sense out of whatever the system comes up with. That's always such a fun challenge for me that I've never ever wondered what HP actually was representing and plainly had fun with that.

      I get to make my descriptions as gory or lighthearted as I want too, in the same vein of your hack, despite doing them after-rolling. Gee, I even make a big part of the description before rolling just like your hack, and then try to make ends meet, with whatever the system says after the rolls. So, while I get what the hack does, since I have always been able to face D&D HP system with the right frame of mind, it makes my imagination fly instead of blocking it, and having intense, detailed, down-to-the-fiction descriptions has always been something I can choose to add or not, depending on the pacing or the importance of the situation; and not a requirement for the mechanic to work.
      \n

That's always such a fun challenge for me that I've never ever wondered what HP actually was representing and plainly had fun with that.
      \nYeah, I love this

      \n

      I've understood that Vincent holds that seeing IIEE as four very distinct things is not productive. Rather it's one big mess of "how to do things".
      But. The weapon physically contacting the fabric as a forceful hit that has happened now. It happened.
      Then there's no point in a RH because you're going to die anyway. B/c you can't survive some sharp blades forcefully slicing into your space suit fabric.
      \nI'm not sure I'm following here. Why is this a problem?

      I mean, if we're fighting in space, or any situation where one hit kills you, then this is perfect, isn't it?

      You're also right about Vincent and IIEE - the I,I,E,E concept is quite an abstraction. Not to be confused with a "real thing", like a law of physics. Still, as we've seen here, such abstractions can sometimes help us communicate with more clarity.
      \n

      Yes, and the spacesuit would have it's own HP and when that HP was depleted, that's when there was initial-and-final contact, that was my thinking.
      \nIndeed. Wouldn't your suggested rule, as I mentioned earlier, bypass the goal of hit points, though?

      I mean, if depleting hit points means you get to dodge hits or get lucky or have the favour of the gods, then why can't I dodge hits in a spacesuit just the same?

      But assigning hit points to the spacesuit and then depleting THOSE means that the wearer's hit points don't matter at all. A weakling 1st-level wizard who is just flailing uselessly will get "punctured" at the same rate as the 20th-level legendary warrior, using his best tricks. (Assuming AC and such is equal, of course.)

      That's the kind of thing that always pops up its head for me in HP-based systems (maybe I'm too fond of fights with multiple objectives and tactical complications!). In this case, I don't understand your objection, I guess. Try again?\n

      I've tried using this metaphor but... it has some other baggage with it. The stomach DID get punched. The arm DID get chopped. We stop time right at the moment where the axe is bearing down on the arm with full force and all the momentum behind it and it's just piercing the skin a little little bit -- time freezes here, and then we roll see what happens.
      It's not a retcon but... it's... all the momentum of the swing might dissipate suddenly.
      \nRight. How is that different from rolling damage and then rolling a "1"? Or even rolling a really high result but having it not matter because the target has lots of HP left?

      Part of the issue here is that I'm comparing the hack with D&D default rules (which are ambiguous about getting hit or not, but strongly imply getting hit with every piece of the puzzle, like armour helping you avoid getting damaged, more damaging weapons dealing more HP loss [as opposed to the hardest to avoid doing the most HP damage), the very nature of terms like "to-hit" and "bloodied" and "dealing damage", "healing" and so forth).

      You, on the other hand, are thinking of your own interpretation, where no one gets hit until the hit points are lost. That, frankly, makes a lot more sense, and solves a lot of these difficulties! However, it's not how most people play.

      \n
      If you don't like this sort of game design, that's understandable. Is there some way that this move takes the fiction into account less than Parley or Carouse, though? It seems very much the same to me.
      \nIn them you add your stat like Dex or Cha, right? Not that I'm the biggest fan of Parley
      \nI'm not, either, but it seems like a fairly standard move. In those, you add Dex or Cha (to simulate ability), yes. In this one, you add for hit points and armour (to simulate resilience), instead.\n
      For instance, let's say I'm trying to hold back a horde of little snarling goblin-creatures at a doorway. If I get hit and I don't choose to "maintain my position", some of them are going to get by. Is that worth taking a blow for?
      \nYou would always choose maintain my position, right?
      \nWhy is that? I don't think *I* would. I would be, rather, weighing the necessities of the moment (keeping the doorway clear) against the potential damage - hence the interesting tactical decision.

      That's why I keep saying that the description of the hits matters here just as much as the damage/HP numbers matter in D&D. In D&D, we could have a rule like, "When you take more than half of your HP in one blow, you're knocked prone." Or I might simply decide, "This is costing me too much HP. I'm gonna retreat, instead."

      In this version, I'm going to judge based on the description of the blow, instead. Getting punched in the stomach? Ok, no problem. I can deal with getting beat up a bit, so long as no one gets through here. However, getting my head chopped off? Hell, no, it's not worth my life (or maybe I decide it is! that would be interesting, too): I'd rather give up my position and take a wound, screw my friends.

      I hope that helps!

      I like hearing about objections or things that feel non-intuitive because it gives me an idea of how well I'm communicating and also where things might be improved, design-wise. I don't think this is Best Rule in the World; it's just an experiment and I'd like to see how far I can take it.
      \n

      \n Indeed (and I'm quite sure I've mentioned this multiple times earlier in the thread), for this to work, getting hurt has to be *rare*. It's a dramatic moment which happens when things go wrong.

      I wouldn't use this in a dungeon crawl game where there are "dozens of hits every day"! Yikes! I think just thinking of that gives me the same mental dissonance you're describing: what does that even mean, unless we're watching a boxing match?

      If it helps to frame it in D&D terms, the mental model I'm starting with here is 1st-level old school D&D (where each character has, say, 1d6ish hit points).\n
      However, it's not how most people play.
      \n"My vision" is actually neither something I came up with on my own nor especially rare. It had already been brought up a couple of times in the thread and I've heard many other groups do the same. The text also supports it explicitly (but with plenty of weird corner cases that seem to be written with the opposite assumption, such as magic missile, how it even works I don't know).

      Fascinating! I've played a lot of D&D and I've never seen this, with any group (in three different countries).

      I'd love to see how healing and healing potions and magic missiles and poisons and so forth are handled in these games.

      I don't think I've ever even seen this in a podcast or Actual Play. For instance, the latest example I can think of the TitansGrave game. They use D&D-style hit points, and it's quite explicit that each "hit" is a "hit" there. (Wil describes the hellions "digging their teeth into" someone's shoulder, for instance, even though the attack only deals 2 points of damage, or maybe even was cancelled out by the PC's armour. That's the standard I've seen across most D&D and D&Dish games. I think I like your version better, weird corner cases notwithstanding!)

      I'd love to see a version of D&D which embraces this explicitly. It should be possible to maneuver someone into an awkward position, thereby reducing their hit points, for instance. Maybe studying an opponent's fighting style could reduce their hit points, as well, since you'll be able to counter a lot of their tricks. Likewise, in some situations you should perhaps be able to strengthen your position - like taking the high ground - thereby regaining some temporary hit points.

      As it reads to me, the system *says* that not all hits are hits in some of the descriptive text, but all the assumptions it makes about bonuses and penalties and such are written from a simulation standpoint. Bigger weapons "deal more damage", more resilient targets have more hit points, bandaging your wounds somehow lets you regain some "luck" of the "favour of the gods", and so on. It would be interesting if taking on disadvantages because your god favours them gave you more hit points, for example: "Ok, I peel off my armour and take off my helmet and I stand before the giant and challenge him to one-on-one combat." "Great, you can feel power fill you as you accept your god's grace - +20 hit points..."

      I wouldn't use this in a dungeon crawl game where there are "dozens of hits every day"! Yikes! I think just thinking of that gives me the same mental dissonance you're describing: what does that even mean, unless we're watching a boxing match?
      \nExactly!
      In my world of course it's just parries and clanging and blocks -- some good (AC prevails, no HP loss), some a little jarring (AC not enough, some HP loss).\n
      As it reads to me, the system *says* that not all hits are hits in some of the descriptive text, but all the assumptions it makes about bonuses and penalties and such are written from a simulation standpoint.
      \nBefore this thread, I would've agreed with you a lot more, I would've said "Yeah, it's boat rules anyway, they just happen to be very fun and good so we cope with the huge dissonnance". But some of the examples that have come up have actually been huge sells for me.

      What most of all comes in mind is the bug swarm vs the huge boulder. To me that was a "Wow, D&D's AC/HP system really is way better than I thought" moment, it made so much sense.

      Like, the huge boulder is more likely to be fight ending if it has any effect at all, the bug swarm is more likely to be tiring and exhausting.

      But it's a dangerous thing to become very sold on a model just because of a couple of very compelling examples. I tend to think of this as "the burning jacket fallacy" because if you're sold on Fate's aspects, suddenly it makes sense to use them for a burning jacket and then you even think it makes sense to use them for everything.

      Another example is the invisible hand of market economics which is so elegant in a couple of cases that it can blind people to some of the problems with market capitalism. Another example of "burning jacket fallacy".

      \n Which ones? In D&D? (Edit: was responding to WarriorMonk above)

      I'd prefer to call it something like "Combat Advantage" or "Combat Reflexes" or some such, depending on what exactly they mean.

      As you point out, Hit Points certainly implies physical hits being made!

      Let me also clarify something:

      I've been defending my hack against various concerns and complaints thus far. However, I don't think it's the Best Hack Ever. It's my first try at a certain type of design. I would welcome any and all improvements, alternate versions, and so on.

      A better version of this (something which hits the same design priorities) would be a great thing.

      For instance, Sandra, having done the math, do you prefer the "spend hit points to add" version or the dice pool version (spend points to add dice to the pool)? (I know your aversion to dice pools, but that doesn't bother me, so hopefully you can somehow factor that out of your answer.)

      \nThey're Hit Points because when they run out, you get Hit
